      <p><b>Zimbabwe</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>), officially the <b>Republic of Zimbabwe</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked country</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>, between the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Limpopo_River" title="Limpopo River">Limpopo Rivers</a>, bordered by <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> to the south, <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a> to the south-west, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> to the north, and <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> to the east. The capital and largest city is <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Harare</a>. The second largest city is <a href="/wiki/Bulawayo" title="Bulawayo">Bulawayo</a>. A country of roughly 15 million people,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Zimbabwe has 16 <a href="/wiki/Official_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Official languages">official languages</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-language_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-language-3">[3]</a></sup> with English, <a href="/wiki/Shona_language" title="Shona language">Shona</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ndebele_language" title="Northern Ndebele language">Ndebele</a> the most common. </p>
      <p>Since the 11th century, present-day Zimbabwe has been the site of several organised states and kingdoms such as the <a href="/wiki/Rozvi" class="mw-redirect" title="Rozvi">Rozvi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mthwakazi" title="Mthwakazi">Mthwakazi</a> kingdoms, as well as being a major route for migration and trade. The <a href="/wiki/British_South_Africa_Company" title="British South Africa Company">British South Africa Company</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes" title="Cecil Rhodes">Cecil Rhodes</a> first <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia_(region)" title="Rhodesia (region)">demarcated</a> the present territory during 1890 when they conquered <a href="/wiki/Mashonaland" title="Mashonaland">Mashonaland</a> and later in 1893 <a href="/wiki/Matabeleland" title="Matabeleland">Matabeleland</a> after a fierce resistance by Matabele people known as the <a href="/wiki/First_Matabele_War" title="First Matabele War">First Matabele War</a>. <a href="/wiki/Company_rule_in_Rhodesia" title="Company rule in Rhodesia">Company rule</a> ended in 1923 with the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Self-governing_colony" title="Self-governing colony">self-governing British colony</a>. In 1965, the conservative <a href="/wiki/White_people_in_Zimbabwe" class="mw-redirect" title="White people in Zimbabwe">white</a> <a href="/wiki/Minority_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Minority rule">minority government</a> <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia%27s_Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Rhodesia's Unilateral Declaration of Independence">unilaterally declared independence</a> as <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a>. The state endured international isolation and a 15-year <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Bush_War" title="Rhodesian Bush War">guerrilla war</a> with <a href="/wiki/Black_nationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Black nationalist">black nationalist</a> forces; this culminated in a <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_House_Agreement" title="Lancaster House Agreement">peace agreement</a> that established universal enfranchisement and <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> sovereignty as Zimbabwe in April 1980. Zimbabwe then joined the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>, from which it was suspended in 2002 for breaches of international law by its then-government under <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a>, and from which it withdrew in December 2003. The <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a> is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Southern_African_Development_Community" title="Southern African Development Community">Southern African Development Community</a> (SADC), the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a> (AU), and the <a href="/wiki/Common_Market_for_Eastern_and_Southern_Africa" title="Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa">Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa</a> (COMESA). It was once known as the "Jewel of Africa" for its great prosperity.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a> became <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Zimbabwe" title="Prime Minister of Zimbabwe">Prime Minister of Zimbabwe</a> in 1980, when his <a href="/wiki/ZANU%E2%80%93PF" title="ZANU–PF">ZANU–PF</a> party won the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesian_general_election,_1980" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Rhodesian general election, 1980">elections</a> following the end of white minority rule; he was the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Zimbabwe" title="President of Zimbabwe">President of Zimbabwe</a> from 1987 until his resignation in 2017. Under Mugabe's <a href="/wiki/Authoritarianism" title="Authoritarianism">authoritarian</a> regime, the state security apparatus dominated the country and was responsible for widespread <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> violations.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The country has been in economic decline since the 1990s, experiencing several crashes and <a href="/wiki/Hyperinflation" title="Hyperinflation">hyperinflation</a> along the way. </p>
      <p>On 15 November 2017, in the wake of over a year of <a href="/wiki/2016%E2%80%9317_Zimbabwe_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2016–17 Zimbabwe protests">protests</a> against his government as well as Zimbabwe's rapidly declining economy, <a href="/wiki/2017_Zimbabwean_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2017 Zimbabwean coup d'état">Mugabe was placed under house arrest</a> by <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_National_Army" title="Zimbabwe National Army">the country's national army</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a> and eventually resigned six days later. <a href="/wiki/Emmerson_Mnangagwa" title="Emmerson Mnangagwa">Emmerson Mnangagwa</a> has since served as Zimbabwe's president. </p> 
      <div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading">

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia_(name)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhodesia (name)">Rhodesia (name)</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The name "Zimbabwe" stems from a <a href="/wiki/Shona_language" title="Shona language">Shona</a> term for <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe</a>, an ancient city in the country's south-east whose remains are now a protected site. Two different theories address the origin of the word. Many sources hold that "Zimbabwe" derives from <i>dzimba-dza-mabwe</i>, translated from the Karanga dialect of Shona as "houses of stones" (<i>dzimba</i> = plural of <i>imba</i>, "house"; <i>mabwe</i> = plural of <i>bwe</i>, "stone").<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The Karanga-speaking Shona people live around Great Zimbabwe in the modern-day <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Zimbabwe" title="Provinces of Zimbabwe">province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Masvingo_Province" title="Masvingo Province">Masvingo</a>. Archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Peter_Garlake" title="Peter Garlake">Peter Garlake</a> claims that "Zimbabwe" represents a contracted form of <i>dzimba-hwe</i>, which means "venerated houses" in the Zezuru dialect of Shona and usually references chiefs' houses or graves.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Zimbabwe was formerly known as <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a> (1898), <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a> (1965), and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Rhodesia" title="Zimbabwe Rhodesia">Zimbabwe Rhodesia</a> (1979). The first recorded use of "Zimbabwe" as a term of national reference dates from 1960 as a coinage by the black nationalist Michael Mawema,<sup id="cite_ref-fontein119120_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fontein119120-22">[22]</a></sup> whose Zimbabwe National Party became the first to officially use the name in 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-ndlovugatsheni113114_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ndlovugatsheni113114-23">[23]</a></sup> The term "Rhodesia"—derived from the surname of <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes" title="Cecil Rhodes">Cecil Rhodes</a>, the primary instigator of British colonisation of the territory during the late 19th century—was perceived by African nationalists as inappropriate because of its colonial origin and connotations.<sup id="cite_ref-fontein119120_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fontein119120-22">[22]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to Mawema, black nationalists held a meeting in 1960 to choose an alternative name for the country, proposing names such as "Matshobana" and "<a href="/wiki/Monomotapa" class="mw-redirect" title="Monomotapa">Monomotapa</a>" before his suggestion, "Zimbabwe", prevailed.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> A further alternative, put forward by nationalists in <a href="/wiki/Matabeleland" title="Matabeleland">Matabeleland</a>, had been "Matopos", referring to the <a href="/wiki/Matobo_National_Park" title="Matobo National Park">Matopos Hills</a> to the south of <a href="/wiki/Bulawayo" title="Bulawayo">Bulawayo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ndlovugatsheni113114_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ndlovugatsheni113114-23">[23]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>It was initially unclear how the chosen term was to be used—a letter written by Mawema in 1961 refers to "Zimbabweland"<sup id="cite_ref-ndlovugatsheni113114_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ndlovugatsheni113114-23">[23]</a></sup> — but "Zimbabwe" was sufficiently established by 1962 to become the generally preferred term of the black nationalist movement.<sup id="cite_ref-fontein119120_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fontein119120-22">[22]</a></sup> In a 2001 interview, black nationalist <a href="/wiki/Edson_Zvobgo" class="mw-redirect" title="Edson Zvobgo">Edson Zvobgo</a> recalled that Mawema mentioned the name during a political rally, "and it caught hold, and that was that".<sup id="cite_ref-fontein119120_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fontein119120-22">[22]</a></sup> The black nationalist factions subsequently used the name during the Second <i><a href="/wiki/Chimurenga" title="Chimurenga">Chimurenga</a></i> campaigns against the Rhodesian government during the <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Bush_War" title="Rhodesian Bush War">Rhodesian Bush War</a> of 1964–1979. Major factions in this camp included the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_National_Union" title="Zimbabwe African National Union">Zimbabwe African National Union</a> (led by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a> from 1975), and the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_People%27s_Union" title="Zimbabwe African People's Union">Zimbabwe African People's Union</a> (led by <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Nkomo" title="Joshua Nkomo">Joshua Nkomo</a> from its founding in the early 1960s).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Zimbabwe" title="History of Zimbabwe">History of Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Before_1887">Before 1887</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Before 1887">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Tower,_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Tower%2C_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg/170px-Tower%2C_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Tower%2C_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg/255px-Tower%2C_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Tower%2C_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg/340px-Tower%2C_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="697" data-file-height="982"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Tower,_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Towers of <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Archaeological records date human settlement of present-day Zimbabwe to at least 100,000 years ago<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. The earliest known inhabitants were probably <a href="/wiki/San_people" title="San people">San people</a>, who left behind arrowheads and cave paintings. The first Bantu-speaking farmers arrived during the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a> around 2,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Societies speaking proto-Shona languages first emerged in the middle Limpopo valley in the 9th century before moving on to the Zimbabwean highlands. The Zimbabwean plateau eventually became the centre of subsequent Shona states, beginning around the 10th century. Around the early 10th century, trade developed with <a href="/wiki/Shirazi_era" title="Shirazi era">Arab merchants</a> on the Indian Ocean coast, helping to develop the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mapungubwe" title="Kingdom of Mapungubwe">Kingdom of Mapungubwe</a> in the 11th century. This was the precursor to the Shona civilisations that would dominate the region during the 13th to 15th centuries, evidenced by ruins at <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Masvingo" title="Masvingo">Masvingo</a>, and by other smaller sites. The main archaeological site uses a unique dry stone architecture. </p>
      <p>The Kingdom of Mapungubwe was the first in a series of trading states which had developed in Zimbabwe by the time the first European explorers arrived from Portugal. These states traded gold, ivory, and copper for cloth and glass.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated241_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated241-27">[27]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>From about 1300 until 1600 the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Zimbabwe" title="Kingdom of Zimbabwe">Kingdom of Zimbabwe</a> eclipsed Mapungubwe. This Shona state further refined and expanded upon Mapungubwe's stone architecture, which survives to this day at the ruins of the kingdom's capital of Great Zimbabwe. From <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1450 to 1760 Zimbabwe gave way to the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mutapa" title="Kingdom of Mutapa">Kingdom of Mutapa</a>. This Shona state ruled much of the area of present-day Zimbabwe, plus parts of central Mozambique. It is known by many names including the <a href="/wiki/Mutapa_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutapa Empire">Mutapa Empire</a>, also known as <i>Mwene Mutapa</i> or <i>Monomotapa</i> as well as "Munhumutapa", and was renowned for its strategic trade routes with the <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> and Portugal. The Portuguese sought to monopolise this influence and began a series of wars which left the empire in near collapse in the early 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated241_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated241-27">[27]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As a direct response to increased European presence in the interior a new Shona state emerged, known as the <a href="/wiki/Rozwi_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Rozwi Empire">Rozwi Empire</a> (1684–1834). Relying on centuries of military, political and religious development, the Rozwi (meaning "destroyers") expelled the Portuguese from the Zimbabwean plateau<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (November 2018)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> by force of arms. They continued the stone-building traditions of the Zimbabwe and Mapungubwe kingdoms while adding <a href="/wiki/Musket" title="Musket">muskets</a> to their arsenal and recruiting a professional army to defend recent conquests.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Harris_-_Ndebelekraal.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Harris_-_Ndebelekraal.png/220px-Harris_-_Ndebelekraal.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Harris_-_Ndebelekraal.png/330px-Harris_-_Ndebelekraal.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Harris_-_Ndebelekraal.png/440px-Harris_-_Ndebelekraal.png 2x" data-file-width="683" data-file-height="533"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Harris_-_Ndebelekraal.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A Matabele <a href="/wiki/Kraal" title="Kraal">kraal</a>, as depicted by <a href="/wiki/William_Cornwallis_Harris" title="William Cornwallis Harris">William Cornwallis Harris</a>, 1836
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Around 1821 the <a href="/wiki/Zulu_people" title="Zulu people">Zulu</a> general <a href="/wiki/Mzilikazi" title="Mzilikazi">Mzilikazi</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Khumalo_clan" title="Khumalo clan">Khumalo clan</a> successfully rebelled against King <a href="/wiki/Shaka" title="Shaka">Shaka</a> and established his own clan, the <a href="/wiki/Ndebele_people_(Zimbabwe)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ndebele people (Zimbabwe)">Ndebele</a>. The Ndebele fought their way northwards into the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Republic" title="South African Republic">Transvaal</a>, leaving a trail of destruction in their wake and beginning an era of widespread devastation known as the <a href="/wiki/Mfecane" title="Mfecane">Mfecane</a>. When <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a> <a href="/wiki/Boers" title="Boers">trekboers</a> converged on the Transvaal in 1836, they drove the tribe even further northward, with the assistance of <a href="/wiki/Tswana_people" title="Tswana people">Tswana</a> <a href="/wiki/Barolong" class="mw-redirect" title="Barolong">Barolong</a> warriors and <a href="/wiki/Griqua_people" title="Griqua people">Griqua</a> <a href="/wiki/Commando" title="Commando">commandos</a>. By 1838 the Ndebele had conquered the Rozwi Empire, along with the other smaller Shona states, and reduced them to <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">vassaldom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-zimstudy_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zimstudy-28">[28]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After losing their remaining South African lands in 1840, Mzilikazi and his tribe permanently settled in the southwest of present-day Zimbabwe in what became known as <a href="/wiki/Matabeleland" title="Matabeleland">Matabeleland</a>, establishing <a href="/wiki/Bulawayo" title="Bulawayo">Bulawayo</a> as their capital. Mzilikazi then organised his society into a military system with regimental <a href="/wiki/Kraal" title="Kraal">kraals</a>, similar to those of Shaka, which was stable enough to repel further Boer incursions. Mzilikazi died in 1868; following a violent power struggle, his son <a href="/wiki/Lobengula" title="Lobengula">Lobengula</a> succeeded him. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Colonial_era_and_Rhodesia_.281888.E2.80.931964.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_era_and_Rhodesia_(1888–1964)">Colonial era and Rhodesia (1888–1964)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Colonial era and Rhodesia (1888–1964)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Company_rule_in_Rhodesia" title="Company rule in Rhodesia">Company rule in Rhodesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Rhodesia_and_Nyasaland" title="Federation of Rhodesia and Nyasaland">Federation of Rhodesia and Nyasaland</a>
      </div> 
      <table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399"></a>
          </div></td>
         <td class="mbox-text">
          <div class="mbox-text-span">
           This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2016</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small>
          </div></td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Hoisting_the_flag_at_Fort_Salisbury.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Hoisting_the_flag_at_Fort_Salisbury.png/220px-Hoisting_the_flag_at_Fort_Salisbury.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Hoisting_the_flag_at_Fort_Salisbury.png/330px-Hoisting_the_flag_at_Fort_Salisbury.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Hoisting_the_flag_at_Fort_Salisbury.png/440px-Hoisting_the_flag_at_Fort_Salisbury.png 2x" data-file-width="852" data-file-height="561"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Hoisting_the_flag_at_Fort_Salisbury.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Union_Jack" title="Union Jack">Union Jack</a> was raised over <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Fort Salisbury</a> on 13 September 1890
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the 1880s European colonists arrived with <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes" title="Cecil Rhodes">Cecil Rhodes</a>'s <a href="/wiki/British_South_Africa_Company" title="British South Africa Company">British South Africa Company</a> (BSAC, incorporated in 1888, chartered in 1889). In 1888 Rhodes obtained a <a href="/wiki/Rudd_Concession" title="Rudd Concession">concession for mining rights</a> from <a href="/wiki/King_Lobengula" class="mw-redirect" title="King Lobengula">King Lobengula</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ndebele_people_(Zimbabwe)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ndebele people (Zimbabwe)">Ndebele</a> peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-mining_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mining-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (July 2020)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup> He presented this concession to persuade the government of the United Kingdom to grant a <a href="/wiki/Royal_charter" title="Royal charter">royal charter</a> to the company over <a href="/wiki/Matabeleland" title="Matabeleland">Matabeleland</a>, and its subject states such as <a href="/wiki/Mashonaland" title="Mashonaland">Mashonaland</a> as well.<sup id="cite_ref-treaties_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treaties-30">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Rhodes used this document in 1890 to justify sending the <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Column" title="Pioneer Column">Pioneer Column</a>, a group of Europeans protected by well-armed <a href="/wiki/British_South_Africa_Police" title="British South Africa Police">British South Africa Police</a> (BSAP) through Matabeleland and into Shona territory to establish Fort Salisbury (present-day <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Harare</a>), and thereby establish <a href="/wiki/Company_rule_in_Rhodesia" title="Company rule in Rhodesia">company rule</a> over the area. In 1893 and 1894, with the help of their new <a href="/wiki/Maxim_gun" title="Maxim gun">Maxim</a> guns, the BSAP would go on to defeat the Ndebele in the <a href="/wiki/First_Matabele_War" title="First Matabele War">First Matabele War</a>. Rhodes additionally sought permission to negotiate similar concessions covering all territory between the <a href="/wiki/Limpopo_River" title="Limpopo River">Limpopo River</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>, then known as "Zambesia".<sup id="cite_ref-treaties_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treaties-30">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In accordance with the terms of aforementioned concessions and treaties,<sup id="cite_ref-treaties_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treaties-30">[30]</a></sup> mass settlement was encouraged,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (July 2020)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> with the British maintaining control over labour as well as over precious metals and other mineral resources.<sup id="cite_ref-bsac_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bsac-31">[31]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_the_Shangani.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Battle_of_the_Shangani.jpg/220px-Battle_of_the_Shangani.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Battle_of_the_Shangani.jpg/330px-Battle_of_the_Shangani.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Battle_of_the_Shangani.jpg/440px-Battle_of_the_Shangani.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1111" data-file-height="698"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_the_Shangani.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Shangani" title="Battle of the Shangani">Battle of the Shangani</a> on 25 October 1893
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1895 the BSAC adopted the name <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia_(name)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhodesia (name)">"Rhodesia"</a> for the territory, in honour of Rhodes. In 1898 "<a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a>" became the official name for the region south of the Zambezi,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-adopted_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adopted-33">[33]</a></sup> which later adopted the name "Zimbabwe". The region to the north, administered separately, was later termed <a href="/wiki/Northern_Rhodesia" title="Northern Rhodesia">Northern Rhodesia</a> (present-day Zambia). Shortly after Rhodes' the disastrous Rhodes-sponsored <a href="/wiki/Jameson_Raid" title="Jameson Raid">Jameson Raid</a> (December 1895 - January 1896) on the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Republic" title="South African Republic">South African Republic</a>, the Ndebele rebelled against white rule, led by their charismatic religious leader, Mlimo. The <a href="/wiki/Second_Matabele_War" title="Second Matabele War">Second Matabele War</a> of 1896-1897 lasted in Matabeleland until 1896, when a British operation had Mlimo assassinated. Shona agitators staged unsuccessful revolts (known as <i><a href="/wiki/Chimurenga" title="Chimurenga">Chimurenga</a></i>) against company rule during 1896 and 1897.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Following these failed insurrections, the Rhodes administration subdued the Ndebele and Shona groups and organised the land with a disproportionate bias favouring Europeans, thus displacing many indigenous peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-sahistory.org.za_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sahistory.org.za-34">[34]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The United Kingdom annexed Southern Rhodesia on 12 September 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Shortly after annexation, on 1 October 1923, the first constitution for the new Colony of Southern Rhodesia came into force.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:SouthernRhodesia2d1953scott83rhodesgrave.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/SouthernRhodesia2d1953scott83rhodesgrave.jpg/170px-SouthernRhodesia2d1953scott83rhodesgrave.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/SouthernRhodesia2d1953scott83rhodesgrave.jpg/255px-SouthernRhodesia2d1953scott83rhodesgrave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/SouthernRhodesia2d1953scott83rhodesgrave.jpg/340px-SouthernRhodesia2d1953scott83rhodesgrave.jpg 2x" data-file-width="646" data-file-height="788"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:SouthernRhodesia2d1953scott83rhodesgrave.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>1953 stamp with the portrait of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Under the new constitution, Southern Rhodesia became a <a href="/wiki/Self-governing_colony" title="Self-governing colony">self-governing</a> <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British colony</a>, subsequent to a <a href="/wiki/1922_Southern_Rhodesian_government_referendum" title="1922 Southern Rhodesian government referendum">1922 referendum</a>. Rhodesians of all races served on behalf of the United Kingdom during the two <a href="/wiki/World_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="World Wars">World Wars</a> in the early-20th century. Proportional to the white population, Southern Rhodesia contributed more <i>per capita</i> to both the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia_in_World_War_I" title="Southern Rhodesia in World War I">First</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia_in_World_War_II" title="Southern Rhodesia in World War II">Second World Wars</a> than any other part of the Empire, including Britain itself.<sup id="cite_ref-moorcraft_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moorcraft-40">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Land_Apportionment_Act_of_1930" title="Land Apportionment Act of 1930">1930 Land Apportionment Act</a> restricted black land ownership to certain segments of the country, setting aside large areas solely for the purchase of the white minority. This act, which led to rapidly rising inequality, became the subject of frequent calls for subsequent land reform.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1953, in the face of African opposition,<sup id="cite_ref-fed_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fed-42">[42]</a></sup> Britain consolidated the two Rhodesias with <a href="/wiki/Nyasaland" title="Nyasaland">Nyasaland</a> (Malawi) in the ill-fated <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Rhodesia_and_Nyasaland" title="Federation of Rhodesia and Nyasaland">Central African Federation</a>, which Southern Rhodesia essentially dominated. Growing <a href="/wiki/African_nationalism" title="African nationalism">African nationalism</a> and general dissent, particularly in Nyasaland, persuaded Britain to dissolve the Union in 1963, forming three separate divisions. While multiracial democracy was finally introduced to Northern Rhodesia and Nyasaland, however, Southern Rhodesians of European ancestry continued to enjoy <a href="/wiki/Minority_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Minority rule">minority rule</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sahistory.org.za_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sahistory.org.za-34">[34]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following <a href="/wiki/Zambian_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Zambian Independence">Zambian independence</a> (effective from October 1964), <a href="/wiki/Ian_Smith" title="Ian Smith">Ian Smith</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Front" title="Rhodesian Front">Rhodesian Front</a> (RF) government in Salisbury dropped the designation "Southern" in 1964 (once <i>Northern Rhodesia</i> had changed its name to <i>Zambia</i>, having the word <i>Southern</i> before the name <i>Rhodesia</i> became unnecessary and the country simply became known as <i>Rhodesia</i> afterwards). Intent on effectively repudiating the recently adopted British policy of "<a href="/wiki/No_independence_before_majority_rule" title="No independence before majority rule">no independence before majority rule</a>", Smith issued a <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia%27s_Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Rhodesia's Unilateral Declaration of Independence">Unilateral Declaration of Independence</a> (commonly abbreviated to "UDI") from the United Kingdom on 11 November 1965. This marked the first such course taken by a rebel British colony since the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">American declaration</a> of 1776, which Smith and others indeed claimed provided a suitable precedent to their own actions.<sup id="cite_ref-moorcraft_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moorcraft-40">[40]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="UDI_and_civil_war_.281965.E2.80.931980.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="UDI_and_civil_war_(1965–1980)">UDI and civil war (1965–1980)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: UDI and civil war (1965–1980)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Bush_War" title="Rhodesian Bush War">Rhodesian Bush War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Rhodesia" title="Zimbabwe Rhodesia">Zimbabwe Rhodesia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_House_Agreement" title="Lancaster House Agreement">Lancaster House Agreement</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Udi2-rho.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/Udi2-rho.jpg/220px-Udi2-rho.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/Udi2-rho.jpg/330px-Udi2-rho.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/a9/Udi2-rho.jpg 2x" data-file-width="348" data-file-height="269"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Udi2-rho.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Ian_Smith" title="Ian Smith">Ian Smith</a> signing the <a href="/wiki/Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence_(Rhodesia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Unilateral Declaration of Independence (Rhodesia)">Unilateral Declaration of Independence</a> on 11 November 1965 with his cabinet in audience.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After the <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia%27s_Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Rhodesia's Unilateral Declaration of Independence">Unilateral Declaration of Independence</a> (UDI), the British government petitioned the United Nations for <a href="/wiki/Economic_sanctions" title="Economic sanctions">sanctions</a> against Rhodesia pending unsuccessful talks with Smith's administration in 1966 and 1968. In December 1966, the organisation complied, imposing the first mandatory trade embargo on an autonomous state.<sup id="cite_ref-eafp_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eafp-43">[43]</a></sup> These sanctions were expanded again in 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-eafp_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eafp-43">[43]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The United Kingdom deemed the Rhodesian declaration an act of rebellion, but did not re-establish control by force. A <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Bush_War" title="Rhodesian Bush War">guerrilla war</a> subsequently ensued when <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Nkomo" title="Joshua Nkomo">Joshua Nkomo</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_People%27s_Union" title="Zimbabwe African People's Union">Zimbabwe African People's Union</a> (ZAPU) and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_National_Union" title="Zimbabwe African National Union">Zimbabwe African National Union</a> (ZANU), supported actively by communist powers and neighbouring African nations, initiated guerrilla operations against Rhodesia's predominantly white government. ZAPU was supported by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> and associated nations such as Cuba, and adopted a <a href="/wiki/Marxism%E2%80%93Leninism" title="Marxism–Leninism">Marxist–Leninist</a> ideology; ZANU meanwhile aligned itself with <a href="/wiki/Maoism" title="Maoism">Maoism</a> and the bloc headed by the People's Republic of China. Smith declared Rhodesia a republic in 1970, following the results of a <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_constitutional_referendum,_1969" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhodesian constitutional referendum, 1969">referendum</a> the previous year, but this went unrecognised internationally. Meanwhile, Rhodesia's internal conflict intensified, eventually forcing him to open negotiations with the militant communists. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Lancaster-House-Agreement.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/36/Lancaster-House-Agreement.png/220px-Lancaster-House-Agreement.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/36/Lancaster-House-Agreement.png/330px-Lancaster-House-Agreement.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/36/Lancaster-House-Agreement.png 2x" data-file-width="374" data-file-height="266"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Lancaster-House-Agreement.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Bishop <a href="/wiki/Abel_Muzorewa" title="Abel Muzorewa">Abel Muzorewa</a> signs the Lancaster House Agreement seated next to <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">British Foreign Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Carington,_6th_Baron_Carrington" title="Peter Carington, 6th Baron Carrington">Lord Carrington</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In March 1978, Smith reached an accord with three African leaders, led by Bishop <a href="/wiki/Abel_Muzorewa" title="Abel Muzorewa">Abel Muzorewa</a>, who offered to leave the white population comfortably entrenched in exchange for the establishment of a biracial democracy. As a result of the <a href="/wiki/Internal_Settlement" title="Internal Settlement">Internal Settlement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Rhodesia_general_election,_1979" class="mw-redirect" title="Zimbabwe Rhodesia general election, 1979">elections</a> were held in April 1979, concluding with the <a href="/wiki/United_African_National_Council" title="United African National Council">United African National Council</a> (UANC) carrying a majority of parliamentary seats. On 1 June 1979, Muzorewa, the UANC head, became prime minister and the country's name was changed to <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Rhodesia" title="Zimbabwe Rhodesia">Zimbabwe Rhodesia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Internal_Settlement" title="Internal Settlement">Internal Settlement</a> left control of the <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Security_Forces" title="Rhodesian Security Forces">Rhodesian Security Forces</a>, civil service, judiciary, and a third of parliament seats to whites.<sup id="cite_ref-endsanctions_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-endsanctions-44">[44]</a></sup> On 12 June, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a> voted to lift economic pressure on the former Rhodesia. </p>
      <p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Heads_of_Government_Meeting_1979" title="Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting 1979">fifth Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting</a> (CHOGM), held in <a href="/wiki/Lusaka" title="Lusaka">Lusaka</a>, Zambia from 1 to 7 August in 1979, the British government invited Muzorewa, Mugabe, and Nkomo to participate in a constitutional conference at <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_House" title="Lancaster House">Lancaster House</a>. The purpose of the conference was to discuss and reach an agreement on the terms of an independence constitution, and provide for elections supervised under British authority allowing Zimbabwe Rhodesia to proceed to legal independence.<sup id="cite_ref-chair_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chair-45">[45]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>With <a href="/wiki/Peter_Carington,_6th_Baron_Carrington" title="Peter Carington, 6th Baron Carrington">Lord Carrington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs</a> of the United Kingdom, in the chair, these discussions were mounted from 10 September to 15 December in 1979, producing a total of 47 <a href="/wiki/Plenary_session" title="Plenary session">plenary sessions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-chair_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chair-45">[45]</a></sup> On 21 December 1979, delegations from every major interest represented reached the <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_House_Agreement" title="Lancaster House Agreement">Lancaster House Agreement</a>, effectively ending the guerrilla war.<sup id="cite_ref-lancaster_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lancaster-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 11 December 1979, the Rhodesian House of Assembly voted 90 to nil to revert to British colonial status (the 'aye' votes included Ian Smith himself). The bill then passed the Senate and was assented to by the President. With the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Soames,_Baron_Soames" class="mw-redirect" title="Christopher Soames, Baron Soames">Lord Soames</a>, the new Governor, just after 2 p.m. on 12 December 1979, Britain formally took control of <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Rhodesia" title="Zimbabwe Rhodesia">Zimbabwe Rhodesia</a> as the Colony of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a>, although on 13 December Soames declared that during his mandate the name Rhodesia and Zimbabwe Rhodesia would continue to be used. Britain lifted sanctions on 12 December, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> on 16 December, before calling on its member states to do likewise on 21 December. Thus Zambia, Mozambique, Tanzania, Angola and Botswana lifted sanctions on 22–23 December; Australia partly pre-empted this, lifting all but trade sanctions on 18 December, and trade sanctions on 21 December.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_parliamentary_election,_1980" class="mw-redirect" title="Zimbabwean parliamentary election, 1980">elections of February 1980</a>, Robert Mugabe and the ZANU party secured a landslide victory.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Prince_Charles" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Charles">Prince Charles</a>, as the representative of Britain, formally granted independence to the new nation of Zimbabwe at a ceremony in <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Harare</a> in April 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Independence_era_.281980.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_era_(1980–present)">Independence era (1980–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Independence era (1980–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Zimbabwe,_Trends_in_the_Human_Development_Index_1970-2010.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Zimbabwe%2C_Trends_in_the_Human_Development_Index_1970-2010.png/350px-Zimbabwe%2C_Trends_in_the_Human_Development_Index_1970-2010.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/58/Zimbabwe%2C_Trends_in_the_Human_Development_Index_1970-2010.png 1.5x" data-file-width="441" data-file-height="256"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Zimbabwe,_Trends_in_the_Human_Development_Index_1970-2010.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Trends in Zimbabwe's <a href="/wiki/Multidimensional_Poverty_Index" title="Multidimensional Poverty Index">Multidimensional Poverty Index</a>, 1970–2010.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_Zimbabwe" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe's first president</a> after its independence was <a href="/wiki/Canaan_Banana" title="Canaan Banana">Canaan Banana</a> in what was originally a mainly ceremonial role as Head of State. Robert Mugabe, leader of the ZANU party, was the country's first Prime Minister and Head of Government.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Opposition to what was perceived as a Shona takeover immediately erupted around <a href="/wiki/Matabeleland" title="Matabeleland">Matabeleland</a>. The Matabele unrest led to what has become known as <i><a href="/wiki/Gukurahundi" title="Gukurahundi">Gukurahundi</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Shona_language" title="Shona language">Shona</a>: <i lang="sn">"the early rain which washes away the <a href="/wiki/Chaff" title="Chaff">chaff</a> before the spring rains"</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-watch_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watch-53">[53]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_Fifth_Brigade" class="mw-redirect" title="Zimbabwean Fifth Brigade">Fifth Brigade</a>, a North Korean-trained elite unit that reported directly to the Zimbabwean Prime Minister,<sup id="cite_ref-meredith6273_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meredith6273-54">[54]</a></sup> entered Matabeleland and massacred thousands of civilians accused of supporting "dissidents".<sup id="cite_ref-meredith6273_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meredith6273-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hill77_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hill77-55">[55]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Estimates for the number of deaths during the five-year <i>Gukurahundi</i> campaign ranged from 3,750<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> to 80,000.<sup id="cite_ref-hill77_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hill77-55">[55]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Thousands of others were tortured in military internment camps.<sup id="cite_ref-catholiccommission_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-catholiccommission-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The campaign officially ended in 1987 after Nkomo and Mugabe reached a unity agreement that merged their respective parties, creating the <a href="/wiki/ZANU%E2%80%93PF" title="ZANU–PF">Zimbabwe African National Union – Patriotic Front</a> (ZANU–PF).<sup id="cite_ref-meredith6273_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meredith6273-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_general_election,_1990" class="mw-redirect" title="Zimbabwean general election, 1990">Elections in March 1990</a> resulted in another victory for Mugabe and the ZANU–PF party, which claimed 117 of the 120 contested seats.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the 1990s, students, <a href="/wiki/Trade_unionist" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade unionist">trade unionists</a>, and other workers often demonstrated to express their growing discontent with Mugabe and ZANU–PF party policies. In 1996, civil servants, nurses, and <a href="/wiki/Junior_doctor" title="Junior doctor">junior doctors</a> went on strike over salary issues.<sup id="cite_ref-l_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-l-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The general health of the population also began to significantly decline; by 1997 an estimated 25% of the population had been infected by HIV in a pandemic that was affecting most of southern Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-k_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Land redistribution re-emerged as the main issue for the <a href="/wiki/ZANU_(PF)" class="mw-redirect" title="ZANU (PF)">ZANU–PF</a> government around 1997. Despite the existence of a "willing-buyer-willing-seller" land reform programme since the 1980s, the minority white Zimbabwean population of around 0.6% continued to hold 70% of the country's most fertile agricultural land.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2000, the government pressed ahead with its <a href="/wiki/Land_reform_in_Zimbabwe#Fast-track_land_reform_and_violence" title="Land reform in Zimbabwe">Fast Track Land Reform</a> programme, a policy involving compulsory land acquisition aimed at redistributing land from the minority white population to the majority black population.<sup id="cite_ref-humanrightswatcha_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-humanrightswatcha-69">[69]</a></sup> Confiscations of white farmland, continuous droughts, and a serious drop in external finance and other supports led to a sharp decline in agricultural exports, which were traditionally the country's leading export-producing sector.<sup id="cite_ref-humanrightswatcha_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-humanrightswatcha-69">[69]</a></sup> Some 58,000 independent black farmers have since experienced limited success in reviving the gutted cash crop sectors through efforts on a smaller scale.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>President Mugabe and the ZANU–PF party leadership found themselves beset by a wide range of international sanctions.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> In 2002, the nation was suspended from the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> due to the reckless farm seizures and blatant <a href="/wiki/Electoral_fraud" title="Electoral fraud">election tampering</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tampering_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tampering-72">[72]</a></sup> The following year, Zimbabwean officials voluntarily terminated its Commonwealth membership.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In 2001, The US enacted the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Democracy_and_Economic_Recovery_Act_of_2001" title="Zimbabwe Democracy and Economic Recovery Act of 2001">Zimbabwe Democracy and Economic Recovery Act</a> (ZDERA). It came into effect in 2002 and froze credit to the Zimbabwean government. The bill was sponsored by <a href="/wiki/Bill_Frist" title="Bill Frist">Bill Frist</a> and co-sponsored by US senators <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Biden" title="Joe Biden">Joe Biden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russ_Feingold" title="Russ Feingold">Russ Feingold</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Helms" title="Jesse Helms">Jesse Helms</a>. Through ZDERA Section 4C ("Multilateral Financing Restriction"), the Secretary of the Treasury is ordered to direct US Directors at the International Financial Institutions listed in Section 3, "to oppose and vote against-- (1) any extension by the respective institution of any loan, credit, or guarantee to the Government of Zimbabwe; or (2) any cancellation or reduction of indebtedness owed by the Government of Zimbabwe to the United States or any international financial institution."<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By 2003, the country's economy had collapsed. It is estimated that up to a quarter of Zimbabwe's 11 million people had fled the country. Three-quarters of the remaining Zimbabweans were living on less than one US dollar a day.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_parliamentary_election,_2005" class="mw-redirect" title="Zimbabwean parliamentary election, 2005">elections in 2005</a>, the government initiated "<a href="/wiki/Operation_Murambatsvina" title="Operation Murambatsvina">Operation Murambatsvina</a>", an effort to crack down on illegal markets and slums emerging in towns and cities, leaving a substantial section of urban poor homeless.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The Zimbabwean government has described the operation as an attempt to provide decent housing to the population, although according to critics such as <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a>, authorities have yet to properly substantiate their claims.<sup id="cite_ref-muram_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-muram-78">[78]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Food_insecurity_in_Zimbabwe.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Food_insecurity_in_Zimbabwe.svg/220px-Food_insecurity_in_Zimbabwe.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Food_insecurity_in_Zimbabwe.svg/330px-Food_insecurity_in_Zimbabwe.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Food_insecurity_in_Zimbabwe.svg/440px-Food_insecurity_in_Zimbabwe.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="630" data-file-height="490"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Food_insecurity_in_Zimbabwe.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Map showing the food insecurity in Zimbabwe in June 2008
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 29 March 2008, Zimbabwe held a <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_presidential_election,_2008" class="mw-redirect" title="Zimbabwean presidential election, 2008">presidential election</a> along with a <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_parliamentary_election,_2008" class="mw-redirect" title="Zimbabwean parliamentary election, 2008">parliamentary election</a>. The results of this election were withheld for two weeks, after which it was generally acknowledged that the <a href="/wiki/Movement_for_Democratic_Change_%E2%80%93_Tsvangirai" title="Movement for Democratic Change – Tsvangirai">Movement for Democratic Change – Tsvangirai</a> (MDC-T) had achieved a majority of one seat in the lower house of parliament.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>On 10 July 2008, Russia and China vetoed UN Zimbabwe sanctions pushed by Britain and the US.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The US drafted the file, which would have placed an arms embargo on Mugabe's regime. However, nine of 15 countries on the UN council opposed it, including Vietnam, South Africa, and Libya, which argued that Zimbabwe was not a 'threat to international peace and security.'<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In late 2008, problems in Zimbabwe reached crisis proportions in the areas of living standards, public health (with a major <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_cholera_outbreak" class="mw-redirect" title="Zimbabwean cholera outbreak">cholera outbreak</a> in December) and various basic affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-Carter_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carter-81">[81]</a></sup> During this period NGOs took over from government as a primary provider of food during this period of food insecurity in Zimbabwe.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In September 2008, a <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932009_Zimbabwean_political_negotiations" title="2008–2009 Zimbabwean political negotiations">power-sharing agreement</a> was reached between Tsvangirai and President Mugabe, permitting the former to hold the office of prime minister. Due to ministerial differences between their respective political parties, the agreement was not fully implemented until 13 February 2009. By December 2010, Mugabe was threatening to completely expropriate remaining privately owned companies in Zimbabwe unless "western sanctions" were lifted.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Mugabe_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Mugabe_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/220px-Mugabe_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Mugabe_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/330px-Mugabe_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Mugabe_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/440px-Mugabe_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg 2x" data-file-width="680" data-file-height="450"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mugabe_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Zimbabwean President <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a> attended the Independence Day celebrations in South Sudan in July 2011
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>A 2011 survey by <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a> suggested that living conditions had improved since the power-sharing agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The United Nations <a href="/wiki/Office_for_the_Coordination_of_Humanitarian_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Office for the Coordination of Humanitarian Affairs">Office for the Coordination of Humanitarian Affairs</a> stated in its 2012–2013 planning document that the "humanitarian situation has improved in Zimbabwe since 2009, but conditions remain precarious for many people".<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 17 January 2013, Vice-President <a href="/wiki/John_Nkomo" title="John Nkomo">John Nkomo</a> died of cancer at St Anne's Hospital, <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Harare</a> at the age of 78.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> A new constitution approved in the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_constitutional_referendum,_2013" class="mw-redirect" title="Zimbabwean constitutional referendum, 2013">Zimbabwean constitutional referendum, 2013</a> curtails presidential powers.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Mugabe was re-elected president in the July 2013 <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_general_election,_2013" class="mw-redirect" title="Zimbabwean general election, 2013">Zimbabwean general election</a> which <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> described as "rigged."<sup id="cite_ref-Economist072016_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist072016-88">[88]</a></sup> and the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">Daily Telegraph</a></i> as "stolen".<sup id="cite_ref-Out_of_House_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Out_of_House-89">[89]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Movement_for_Democratic_Change_%E2%80%93_Tsvangirai" title="Movement for Democratic Change – Tsvangirai">Movement for Democratic Change</a> alleged massive fraud and tried to seek relief through the courts.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> In a surprising moment of candour at the ZANU–PF congress in December 2014, President Robert Mugabe accidentally let slip that the opposition had in fact won the contentious 2008 polls by an astounding 73%.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> After winning the election, the Mugabe ZANU–PF government re-instituted <a href="/wiki/One_party_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="One party rule">one party rule</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Out_of_House_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Out_of_House-89">[89]</a></sup> doubled the civil service and, according to <i>The Economist</i>, embarked on "misrule and dazzling corruption".<sup id="cite_ref-Economist072016_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist072016-88">[88]</a></sup> A 2017 study conducted by the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Security_Studies" title="Institute for Security Studies">Institute for Security Studies</a> (ISS) concluded that due to the deterioration of government and the economy "the government encourages corruption to make up for its inability to fund its own institutions" with widespread and informal police roadblocks to issue fines to travellers being one manifestation of this.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In July 2016 <a href="/wiki/2016_Zimbabwe_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2016 Zimbabwe protests">nationwide protests</a> took place regarding the economic collapse in the country,<sup id="cite_ref-BBC2016_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2016-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> and the finance minister admitted "Right now we literally have nothing."<sup id="cite_ref-Economist072016_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist072016-88">[88]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In November 2017, <a href="/wiki/2017_Zimbabwean_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2017 Zimbabwean coup d'état">the army led a coup d'état</a> following the dismissal of Vice-President <a href="/wiki/Emmerson_Mnangagwa" title="Emmerson Mnangagwa">Emmerson Mnangagwa</a>, placing Mugabe under house arrest. The army denied that their actions constituted a <a href="/wiki/Coup" class="mw-redirect" title="Coup">coup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn_turmoil_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn_turmoil-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-guardian_confined_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian_confined-96">[96]</a></sup> On 19 November 2017, ZANU–PF sacked Robert Mugabe as party leader and appointed former Vice-President <a href="/wiki/Emmerson_Mnangagwa" title="Emmerson Mnangagwa">Emmerson Mnangagwa</a> in his place.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> On 21 November 2017, Mugabe tendered his resignation prior to impeachment proceedings being completed.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_resigns_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_resigns-98">[98]</a></sup> Although under the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Zimbabwe" title="Constitution of Zimbabwe">Constitution of Zimbabwe</a> Mugabe should be succeeded by Vice-President <a href="/wiki/Phelekezela_Mphoko" title="Phelekezela Mphoko">Phelekezela Mphoko</a>, a supporter of <a href="/wiki/Grace_Mugabe" title="Grace Mugabe">Grace Mugabe</a>, ZANU–PF chief whip <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lovemore_Matuke&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lovemore Matuke (page does not exist)">Lovemore Matuke</a> stated to the <a href="/wiki/Reuters" title="Reuters">Reuters</a> news agency that Mnangagwa would be appointed as president.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_resigns_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_resigns-98">[98]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 30 July 2018 Zimbabwe held its <a href="/wiki/2018_Zimbabwean_general_election" title="2018 Zimbabwean general election">general elections</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> which were won by the ZANU-PF party led by Mnangagwa.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Chamisa" title="Nelson Chamisa">Nelson Chamisa</a> who was leading the main opposition party <a href="/wiki/MDC_Alliance" class="mw-redirect" title="MDC Alliance">MDC Alliance</a> contested the election results and filed a petition to the Constitution Court of Zimbabwe.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The court confirmed Mnangagwa's victory, making him the newly elected president after Mugabe.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In December 2017 the website Zimbabwe News, calculating the cost of the Mugabe era using various statistics, said that at the time of independence in 1980, the country was growing economically at about five per cent a year, and had done so for quite a long time. If this rate of growth had been maintained for the next 37 years, Zimbabwe would have in 2016 a GDP of US$52 billion. Instead it had a formal sector GDP of only US$14 billion, a cost of US$38 billion in lost growth. The population growth in 1980 was among the highest in Africa at about 3.5 per cent per annum, doubling every 21 years. Had this growth been maintained, the population would have been 31 million. Instead, as of 2018, it is about 13 million. The discrepancies were believed to be partly caused by death from starvation and disease, and partly due to decreased fertility. The life expectancy has halved, and death from politically motivated violence sponsored by government exceeds 200,000 since 1980. The Mugabe government has directly or indirectly caused the deaths of at least three million Zimbabweans in 37 years.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> According to World Food Programme, over two million people are facing starvation because of the recent droughts the country is going through.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Zimbabwe" title="Geography of Zimbabwe">Geography of Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:ZmbziRvr.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/ZmbziRvr.jpg/220px-ZmbziRvr.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/ZmbziRvr.jpg/330px-ZmbziRvr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/ZmbziRvr.jpg/440px-ZmbziRvr.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:ZmbziRvr.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Zambezi_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Zambezi River">Zambezi River</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Mana_Pools_National_Park" title="Mana Pools National Park">Mana Pools National Park</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Zimbabwe_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Zimbabwe_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/220px-Zimbabwe_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Zimbabwe_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/330px-Zimbabwe_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Zimbabwe_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/440px-Zimbabwe_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1900" data-file-height="1900"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Zimbabwe_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Zimbabwe map of Köppen climate classification.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Zimbabwe is a landlocked country in southern Africa, lying between latitudes <a href="/wiki/15th_parallel_south" title="15th parallel south">15°</a> and <a href="/wiki/23rd_parallel_south" title="23rd parallel south">23°S</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/25th_meridian_east" title="25th meridian east">25°</a> and <a href="/wiki/34th_meridian_east" title="34th meridian east">34°E</a>. It is bordered by South Africa to the south, Botswana to the west and southwest, Zambia to the northwest, and Mozambique to the east and northeast. Its northwest corner is roughly 150 meters from Namibia, nearly forming a four-nation <a href="/wiki/Quadripoint" title="Quadripoint">quadripoint</a>. Most of the country is elevated, consisting of a central plateau (high veld) stretching from the southwest northwards with altitudes between 1,000 and 1,600 m. The country's extreme east is mountainous, this area being known as the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Highlands" title="Eastern Highlands">Eastern Highlands</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Mount_Nyangani" title="Mount Nyangani">Mount Nyangani</a> as the highest point at 2,592 m.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The highlands are known for their natural environment, with tourist destinations such as Nyanga, Troutbeck, Chimanimani, Vumba and Chirinda Forest at Mount Selinda. About 20% of the country consists of low-lying areas, (the low veld) under 900m. <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Falls,_Zambia" class="mw-redirect" title="Victoria Falls, Zambia">Victoria Falls</a>, one of the world's biggest and most spectacular waterfalls, is located in the country's extreme northwest and is part of the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a> river.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Zimbabwe" title="Geology of Zimbabwe">Geology of Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Over geological time Zimbabwe has experienced two major post-Gondwana erosion cycles (known as African and post-African), and a very subordinate Plio-Pleistocene cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Zimbabwe has a tropical climate with many local variations. The southern areas are known for their heat and aridity, parts of the central plateau receive frost in winter, the Zambezi valley is also known for its extreme heat and the Eastern Highlands usually experience cool temperatures and the highest rainfall in the country. The country's rainy season generally runs from late October to March and the hot climate is moderated by increasing altitude. Zimbabwe is faced with recurring droughts. The most recent one began early in 2015 and lasted into 2016.
