        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Zimbabwe" title="Tourism in Zimbabwe">Tourism in Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Victoria5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Victoria5.jpg/300px-Victoria5.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Victoria5.jpg/450px-Victoria5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Victoria5.jpg/600px-Victoria5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="512"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Victoria5.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Victoria_Falls" title="Victoria Falls">Victoria Falls</a>, the end of the upper <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a> and beginning of the middle Zambezi.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Land_reform_in_Zimbabwe" title="Land reform in Zimbabwe">land reform programme</a> in 2000, tourism in Zimbabwe has steadily declined. But in 2018, 2.6 million tourists arrived in Zimbabwe, which is the peak level till now.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2016, the total contribution of tourism to Zimbabwe was $1.1 billion (USD), or about 8.1% of Zimbabwe's GDP. It is expected to rise 1.4% in 2017. Employment in travel and tourism, as well as industries travel and tourism indirectly supports, was 5.2% of national employment and is expected to rise by 1.4% in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Several airlines pulled out of Zimbabwe between 2000 and 2007. Australia's <a href="/wiki/Qantas" title="Qantas">Qantas</a>, Germany's <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Airlines" title="Austrian Airlines">Austrian Airlines</a> were among the first to pull out and in 2007 <a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">British Airways</a> suspended all direct flights to Harare.<sup id="cite_ref-tourism_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tourism-210">[210]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> The country's flagship airline <a href="/wiki/Air_Zimbabwe" title="Air Zimbabwe">Air Zimbabwe</a>, which operated flights throughout Africa and a few destinations in Europe and Asia, ceased operations in February 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (August 2017)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> As of 2017, several major commercial airlines had resumed flights to Zimbabwe. </p>
      <p>Zimbabwe has several major tourist attractions. <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Falls,_Zambia" class="mw-redirect" title="Victoria Falls, Zambia">Victoria Falls</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a>, which are shared with Zambia, are located in the north west of Zimbabwe. Before the economic changes, much of the tourism for these locations came to the Zimbabwe side but now Zambia is the main beneficiary. The <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Falls_National_Park" title="Victoria Falls National Park">Victoria Falls National Park</a> is also in this area and is one of the eight main <a href="/wiki/National_parks" class="mw-redirect" title="National parks">national parks</a> in Zimbabwe,<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> the largest of which is <a href="/wiki/Hwange_National_Park" title="Hwange National Park">Hwange National Park</a>. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Highlands" title="Eastern Highlands">Eastern Highlands</a> are a series of mountainous areas near the border with Mozambique. The highest peak in Zimbabwe, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Nyangani" title="Mount Nyangani">Mount Nyangani</a> at 2,593&nbsp;m (8,507&nbsp;ft) is located here as well as the <a href="/wiki/Bvumba_Mountains" title="Bvumba Mountains">Bvumba Mountains</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nyanga_National_Park" title="Nyanga National Park">Nyanga National Park</a>. <a href="/wiki/World%27s_View,_Nyanga" title="World's View, Nyanga">World's View</a> is in these mountains and it is from here that places as far away as 60–70&nbsp;km (37–43&nbsp;mi) are visible and, on clear days, the town of <a href="/wiki/Rusape" title="Rusape">Rusape</a> can be seen. </p>
      <p>Zimbabwe is unusual in Africa in that there are a number of ancient ruined cities built in a unique <a href="/wiki/Dry_stone" title="Dry stone">dry stone</a> style. Among the most famous of these are the <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe</a> ruins in <a href="/wiki/Masvingo" title="Masvingo">Masvingo</a>. Other ruins include <a href="/wiki/Khami" title="Khami">Khami Ruins, Zimbabwe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dhlo-Dhlo" class="mw-redirect" title="Dhlo-Dhlo">Dhlo-Dhlo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naletale" title="Naletale">Naletale</a>. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Matobo_National_Park" title="Matobo National Park">Matobo Hills</a> are an area of <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> <a href="/wiki/Kopje" class="mw-redirect" title="Kopje">kopjes</a> and wooded valleys commencing some 35&nbsp;km (22&nbsp;mi) south of <a href="/wiki/Bulawayo" title="Bulawayo">Bulawayo</a> in southern Zimbabwe. The Hills were formed over 2,000 million years ago with granite being forced to the surface, then being eroded to produce smooth "whaleback dwalas" and broken kopjes, strewn with boulders and interspersed with thickets of vegetation. <a href="/wiki/Mzilikazi" title="Mzilikazi">Mzilikazi</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Ndebele_people_(Zimbabwe)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ndebele people (Zimbabwe)">Ndebele</a> nation, gave the area its name, meaning 'Bald Heads'. They have become a tourist attraction due to their ancient shapes and local wildlife. <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes" title="Cecil Rhodes">Cecil Rhodes</a> and other early white pioneers like <a href="/wiki/Leander_Starr_Jameson" title="Leander Starr Jameson">Leander Starr Jameson</a> are buried in these hills at a site named World's View.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_supply_and_sanitation">Water supply and sanitation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Water supply and sanitation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_Zimbabwe" title="Water supply and sanitation in Zimbabwe">Water supply and sanitation in Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">Water supply</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> in Zimbabwe is defined by many small scale successful programs but also by a general lack of improved water and <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> systems for the majority of Zimbabwe. According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> in 2012, 80% of Zimbabweans had access to improved, i.e. clean, <a href="/wiki/Drinking-water" class="mw-redirect" title="Drinking-water">drinking-water</a> sources, and only 40% of Zimbabweans had access to improved sanitation facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> Access to improved water supply and sanitation is distinctly less in rural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-wsp1_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsp1-216">[216]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There are many factors which continue to determine the nature, for the foreseeable future, of water supply and sanitation in Zimbabwe. </p>
      <p>Three major factors are the severely depressed state of the Zimbabwean economy, the reluctance of foreign aid organisations to build and finance infrastructure projects, and the political instability of the Zimbabwean state.<sup id="cite_ref-wsp1_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsp1-216">[216]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hrw2_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hrw2-217">[217]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Science and technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_Zimbabwe" title="Science and technology in Zimbabwe">Science and technology in Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Life_sciences_and_geosciences_dominate,_Cumulative_totals_by_field,_2008%E2%80%932014.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Life_sciences_and_geosciences_dominate%2C_Cumulative_totals_by_field%2C_2008%E2%80%932014.svg/220px-Life_sciences_and_geosciences_dominate%2C_Cumulative_totals_by_field%2C_2008%E2%80%932014.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Life_sciences_and_geosciences_dominate%2C_Cumulative_totals_by_field%2C_2008%E2%80%932014.svg/330px-Life_sciences_and_geosciences_dominate%2C_Cumulative_totals_by_field%2C_2008%E2%80%932014.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Life_sciences_and_geosciences_dominate%2C_Cumulative_totals_by_field%2C_2008%E2%80%932014.svg/440px-Life_sciences_and_geosciences_dominate%2C_Cumulative_totals_by_field%2C_2008%E2%80%932014.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="511" data-file-height="292"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Life_sciences_and_geosciences_dominate,_Cumulative_totals_by_field,_2008%E2%80%932014.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Scientific research output in terms of publications in Southern Africa, cumulative totals by field, 2008–2014. Source: UNESCO Science Report: towards 2030 (2015), Figure 20.6
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Zimbabwe has relatively well-developed national infrastructure and a long-standing tradition of promoting research and development (R&amp;D), as evidenced by the levy imposed on tobacco-growers since the 1930s to promote market research.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-218">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-219">[219]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The country also has a well-developed education system, with one in 11 adults holding a tertiary degree. Given the country's solid knowledge base and abundant natural resources, Zimbabwe has the potential to figure among the countries leading growth in sub-Saharan Africa by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-218">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-219">[219]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>To do so, however, Zimbabwe will need to correct a number of structural weaknesses. For instance, it lacks the critical mass of researchers needed to trigger innovation. Although the infrastructure is in place to harness research and development to Zimbabwe's socio-economic development, universities and research institutions lack the financial and human resources to conduct research and the regulatory environment hampers the transfer of new technologies to the business sector. The economic crisis has precipitated an exodus of university students and professionals in key areas of expertise (medicine, engineering, etc.) that is of growing concern. More than 22% of Zimbabwean tertiary students were completing their degrees abroad in 2012, compared to a 4% average for sub-Saharan Africa as a whole. In 2012, there were 200 researchers (head count) employed in the public sector, one-quarter of whom were women. This is double the continental average (91 in 2013) but only one-quarter the researcher density of South Africa (818 per million inhabitants). The government has created the Zimbabwe Human Capital Website to provide information for the diaspora on job and investment opportunities in Zimbabwe.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_218-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-218">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_219-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-219">[219]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Scientific_publication_trends_in_SADC_countries,_2005-2014.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Scientific_publication_trends_in_SADC_countries%2C_2005-2014.svg/220px-Scientific_publication_trends_in_SADC_countries%2C_2005-2014.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Scientific_publication_trends_in_SADC_countries%2C_2005-2014.svg/330px-Scientific_publication_trends_in_SADC_countries%2C_2005-2014.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Scientific_publication_trends_in_SADC_countries%2C_2005-2014.svg/440px-Scientific_publication_trends_in_SADC_countries%2C_2005-2014.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="595" data-file-height="589"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Scientific_publication_trends_in_SADC_countries,_2005-2014.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Scientific publication trends in the most productive SADC countries, 2005–2014. Source: UNESCO Science Report: towards 2030 (2015), data from Thomson Reuters' Web of Science, Science Citation Index Expanded
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Despite the fact that human resources are a pillar of any research and innovation policy, the <i>Medium Term Plan</i> <i>2011–2015</i> did not discuss any explicit policy for promoting postgraduate studies in science and engineering. The scarcity of new PhDs in science and engineering fields from the University of Zimbabwe in 2013 was symptomatic of this omission.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_218-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-218">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_219-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-219">[219]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Nor does the development agenda to 2018, the <i>Zimbabwe Agenda for Sustainable Economic Transformation,</i> contain any specific targets for increasing the number of scientists and engineers, or the staffing requirements for industry and other productive sectors. In addition, the lack of co-ordination and coherence among governance structures has led to a multiplication of research priorities and poor implementation of existing policies.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_218-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-218">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_219-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-219">[219]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The country's <i>Second Science and Technology Policy</i> was launched in June 2012, after being elaborated with UNESCO assistance. It replaces the earlier policy dating from 2002. The 2012 policy prioritises biotechnology, information and communication technologies (ICTs), space sciences, nanotechnology, indigenous knowledge systems, technologies yet to emerge and scientific solutions to emergent environmental challenges. The <i>Second Science and Technology Policy</i> also asserts the government commitment to allocating at least 1% of GDP to research and development, focusing at least 60% of university education on developing skills in science and technology and ensuring that school pupils devote at least 30% of their time to studying science subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_218-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-218">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_219-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-219">[219]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2014, Zimbabwe counted 21 publications per million inhabitants in internationally catalogued journals, according to Thomson Reuters' Web of Science (Science Citation Index Expanded). This placed Zimbabwe sixth out of the 15 SADC countries, behind Namibia (59), Mauritius (71), Botswana (103) and, above all, South Africa (175) and the Seychelles (364). The average for sub-Saharan Africa was 20 scientific publications per million inhabitants, compared to a global average of 176 per million.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_219-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-219">[219]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Zimbabwe" title="Demographics of Zimbabwe">Demographics of Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-left: 10px"> 
       <caption>
        Population in Zimbabwe<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-220">[220]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2019_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2019-221">[221]</a></sup> 
       </caption>

        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>2018</td> 
         <td style="text-align:right;">14.4 </td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Shona_witch_doctor_(Zimbabwe).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Shona_witch_doctor_%28Zimbabwe%29.jpg/170px-Shona_witch_doctor_%28Zimbabwe%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Shona_witch_doctor_%28Zimbabwe%29.jpg/255px-Shona_witch_doctor_%28Zimbabwe%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Shona_witch_doctor_%28Zimbabwe%29.jpg/340px-Shona_witch_doctor_%28Zimbabwe%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1642" data-file-height="2400"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Shona_witch_doctor_(Zimbabwe).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A <a href="/wiki/N%27anga" class="mw-redirect" title="N'anga">n'anga</a> (Traditional Healer) of the <a href="/wiki/Shona_people" title="Shona people">Shona people</a>, holding a <a href="/wiki/Kudu" title="Kudu">kudu</a> horn trumpet
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Zimbabwe's total population is 12.97&nbsp;million.<sup id="cite_ref-zimstat1_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zimstat1-8">[8]</a></sup> According to the United Nations <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>, the life expectancy for men was 56 years and the life expectancy for women was 60 years of age (2012).<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> In 2006 an association of doctors in Zimbabwe made calls for then-President Mugabe to make moves to assist the ailing health service.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_Zimbabwe" title="HIV/AIDS in Zimbabwe">HIV infection rate in Zimbabwe</a> was estimated to be 14% for people aged 15–49 in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-UNAIDS_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNAIDS-224">[224]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> reported a decline in HIV prevalence among pregnant women from 26% in 2002 to 21% in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-UNESCO_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO-225">[225]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Some 85% of Zimbabweans are Christian; 62% of the population attends religious services regularly.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> The largest Christian churches are <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a>, Roman Catholic, <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventist_Church" title="Seventh-day Adventist Church">Seventh-day Adventist</a><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Methodist" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodist">Methodist</a>. </p>
      <p>As in other African countries, Christianity may be mixed with enduring traditional beliefs. <a href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancestor worship">Ancestral worship</a> is the most practised non-Christian religion, involving <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> intercession; the <a href="/wiki/Mbira" title="Mbira">mbira dzavadzimu</a>, which means "voice of the ancestors", an instrument related to many <a href="/wiki/Lamellophone" title="Lamellophone">lamellophones</a> ubiquitous throughout Africa, is central to many ceremonial proceedings. Mwari simply means "God the Creator" (musika vanhu in Shona). Around 1% of the population is Muslim.<sup id="cite_ref-US_state_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US_state-228">[228]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Tonga_wall_baskets_producer_from_Zimbabwe.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Tonga Woman" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Tonga_wall_baskets_producer_from_Zimbabwe.JPG/220px-Tonga_wall_baskets_producer_from_Zimbabwe.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Tonga_wall_baskets_producer_from_Zimbabwe.JPG/330px-Tonga_wall_baskets_producer_from_Zimbabwe.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Tonga_wall_baskets_producer_from_Zimbabwe.JPG/440px-Tonga_wall_baskets_producer_from_Zimbabwe.JPG 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Tonga_wall_baskets_producer_from_Zimbabwe.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A Tonga woman pleating a basket
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Ethnic groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Bantu-speaking ethnic groups make up 98% of the population. The majority people, the <a href="/wiki/Shona_people" title="Shona people">Shona</a>, comprise 70%. The <a href="/wiki/Ndebele_people_(Zimbabwe)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ndebele people (Zimbabwe)">Ndebele</a> are the second most populous with 20% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-ethnic_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnic-229">[229]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Source does not give percentages (September 2020)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-230">[230]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="Dead link to an unreliable source that cites and unspecified page that they say 'appears' to have taken their info from a 1995 estimate by the CIA world factbook, but which is not present in the current factbook (September 2020)">better&nbsp;source&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Ndebele descended from <a href="/wiki/Zulu_people" title="Zulu people">Zulu</a> migrations in the 19th century and the other tribes with which they intermarried. Up to one million Ndebele may have left the country over the last five years, mainly for South Africa. Other <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a> ethnic groups make up the third largest with 2 to 5%: these are <a href="/wiki/Venda" title="Venda">Venda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tonga_language_(Zambia_and_Zimbabwe)" title="Tonga language (Zambia and Zimbabwe)">Tonga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shangaan" class="mw-redirect" title="Shangaan">Shangaan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalanga_language" title="Kalanga language">Kalanga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sotho_language" title="Sotho language">Sotho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ndau_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ndau language">Ndau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nambya" class="mw-redirect" title="Nambya">Nambya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tswana_language" title="Tswana language">Tswana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_language" title="Xhosa language">Xhosa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lozi_language" title="Lozi language">Lozi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ethnic_229-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnic-229">[229]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Source does not mention any of this (September 2020)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Minority ethnic groups include <a href="/wiki/White_people_in_Zimbabwe" class="mw-redirect" title="White people in Zimbabwe">white Zimbabweans</a>, who make up less than 1% of the total population. White Zimbabweans are mostly of British origin, but there are also <a href="/wiki/Afrikaner" class="mw-redirect" title="Afrikaner">Afrikaner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Zimbabwe" title="Greeks in Zimbabwe">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_people" title="Portuguese people">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franco-Mauritian" class="mw-redirect" title="Franco-Mauritian">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a> communities. The white population dropped from a peak of around 278,000 or 4.3% of the population in 1975<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> to possibly 120,000 in 1999, and was estimated to be no more than 50,000 in 2002, and possibly much less. The 2012 census lists the total white population at 28,782 (roughly 0.22% of the population), one-twentieth of its 1975 estimated size.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> Most emigration has been to the United Kingdom (between <a href="/wiki/Zimbabweans_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Zimbabweans in the United Kingdom">200,000 and 500,000</a> Britons are of Rhodesian or Zimbabwean origin), South Africa, Botswana, <a href="/wiki/Zimbabweans_in_Zambia" title="Zimbabweans in Zambia">Zambia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> Mozambique,<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> Canada, Australia and New Zealand. <a href="/wiki/Coloureds" title="Coloureds">Coloureds</a> form 0.5% of the population, and various <a href="/wiki/Asian_people" title="Asian people">Asian</a> ethnic groups, mostly of Indian and Chinese origin, are also 0.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to 2012 Census report, 99.7% of the population is of African origin.<sup id="cite_ref-zimstat.co.zw_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zimstat.co.zw-236">[236]</a></sup> Official fertility rates over the last decade were 3.6 (2002 Census),<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> 3.8 (2006)<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> and 3.8 (2012 Census).<sup id="cite_ref-zimstat.co.zw_236-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zimstat.co.zw-236">[236]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><br> </p>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refugee_crisis">Refugee crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Refugee crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The economic meltdown and repressive political measures in Zimbabwe have led to a flood of refugees into neighbouring countries. An estimated 3.4 million Zimbabweans, a quarter of the population, had fled abroad by mid-2007.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> Some 3,000,000 of these left for South Africa and Botswana.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Apart from the people who fled into the neighbouring countries, there are approximately 36,000 internally displaced persons (IDPs). There is no comprehensive survey,<sup id="cite_ref-IDMC_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IDMC-242">[242]</a></sup> although the following figures are available: </p> 
      <table class="wikitable table"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <th>Survey </th> 
         <th>Number </th> 
         <th>Date </th> 
         <th>Source </th>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>National Survey </td> 
         <td>880–960,000 </td> 
         <td>2007 </td> 
         <td>Zimbabwe Vulnerability Assessment Committee<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>Former Farm Workers </td> 
         <td>1,000,000 </td> 
         <td>2008 </td> 
         <td>UNDP<sup id="cite_ref-IDMC_242-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IDMC-242">[242]</a></sup> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>Victims of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Murambatsvina" title="Operation Murambatsvina">Operation Murambatsvina</a> </td> 
         <td>570,000 </td> 
         <td>2005 </td> 
         <td>UN<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup> </td>
        </tr>

        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>The above surveys do not include people displaced by Operation Chikorokoza Chapera or beneficiaries of the fast-track land reform programme but who have since been evicted.<sup id="cite_ref-IDMC_242-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IDMC-242">[242]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Zimbabwe" title="Languages of Zimbabwe">Languages of Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <p>English is the main language used in the education and judicial systems. The <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a> <a href="/wiki/Shona_language" title="Shona language">Shona</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ndebele_language" title="Northern Ndebele language">Ndebele</a> are the principal indigenous languages of Zimbabwe. <a href="/wiki/Shona_language" title="Shona language">Shona</a> is spoken by 70% of the population, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ndebele_language" title="Northern Ndebele language">Ndebele</a> by 20%. Other minority Bantu languages include <a href="/wiki/Venda" title="Venda">Venda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tsonga_language" title="Tsonga language">Tsonga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shangaan" class="mw-redirect" title="Shangaan">Shangaan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalanga_language" title="Kalanga language">Kalanga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sotho_language" title="Sotho language">Sotho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ndau_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ndau language">Ndau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nambya" class="mw-redirect" title="Nambya">Nambya</a>. Less than 2.5%, mainly the white and "<a href="/wiki/Coloureds" title="Coloureds">coloured</a>" (mixed race) minorities, consider English their native language.<sup id="cite_ref-languages_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-languages-245">[245]</a></sup> Shona has a rich oral tradition, which was incorporated into the first Shona novel, <i>Feso</i> by <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Mutswairo" title="Solomon Mutswairo">Solomon Mutswairo</a>, published in 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-feso_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feso-246">[246]</a></sup> English is spoken primarily in the cities, but less so in rural areas. Radio and television news now broadcast in Shona, Sindebele and English.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Due to its large border with Mozambique, there is a large community of Portuguese speakers in Zimbabwe, mainly in the border areas with Mozambique and in major cities, such as <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Harare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bulawayo" title="Bulawayo">Bulawayo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> Beginning in 2017, teaching Portuguese was included in secondary education of Zimbabwe.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Zimbabwe has 16 official languages and under the constitution, an Act of Parliament may prescribe other languages as officially recognised languages.<sup id="cite_ref-language_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-language-3">[3]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religions">Religions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Religions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Zimbabwe" title="Religion in Zimbabwe">Religion in Zimbabwe</a>
      </div>
