      </div>
      <p>According to the 2017 Inter Censal Demography Survey by the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_National_Statistics_Agency" title="Zimbabwe National Statistics Agency">Zimbabwe National Statistics Agency</a> 69.2 per cent of Zimbabweans belong to <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> Christianity, 8.0 per cent are <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a>, in total 84.1 per cent follow one of the denominations of Christianity. 10.2 per cent of the population does not belong to any religion, while the 0.7 per cent is <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2017survey_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2017survey-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pewzimb_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pewzimb-250">[250]</a></sup> </p>
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Zimbabwe" title="Culture of Zimbabwe">Culture of Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Zimbabwe has many different cultures which may include <a href="/wiki/Beliefs" class="mw-redirect" title="Beliefs">beliefs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ceremonies" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceremonies">ceremonies</a>, one of them being <a href="/wiki/Shona_people" title="Shona people">Shona</a>, Zimbabwe's largest ethnic group. The Shona people have many sculptures and <a href="/wiki/Rock_carving" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock carving">carvings</a> which are made with the finest materials available.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Zimbabwe first celebrated its independence on 18 April 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-indepday_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-indepday-252">[252]</a></sup> Celebrations are held at either the <a href="/wiki/National_Sports_Stadium_(Zimbabwe)" title="National Sports Stadium (Zimbabwe)">National Sports Stadium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rufaro_Stadium" title="Rufaro Stadium">Rufaro Stadium</a> in Harare. The first independence celebrations were held in 1980 at the Zimbabwe Grounds. At these celebrations, <a href="/wiki/Dove" class="mw-redirect" title="Dove">doves</a> are released to symbolise peace and fighter jets fly over and the <a href="/wiki/National_Anthem_of_Zimbabwe" title="National Anthem of Zimbabwe">national anthem</a> is sung. The flame of independence is lit by the president after parades by the presidential family and members of the armed forces of Zimbabwe. The president also gives a speech to the people of Zimbabwe which is televised for those unable to attend the stadium.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> Zimbabwe also has a national beauty pageant, the <a href="/wiki/Miss_Heritage_Zimbabwe" title="Miss Heritage Zimbabwe">Miss Heritage Zimbabwe</a> contest which has been held annually ever since 2012. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts">Arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_art" title="Zimbabwean art">Zimbabwean art</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Zimbabwe" title="Music of Zimbabwe">Music of Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Reconciliation_Amos_Supuni_Woerden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Reconciliation_Amos_Supuni_Woerden.jpg/220px-Reconciliation_Amos_Supuni_Woerden.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="318" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Reconciliation_Amos_Supuni_Woerden.jpg/330px-Reconciliation_Amos_Supuni_Woerden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Reconciliation_Amos_Supuni_Woerden.jpg/440px-Reconciliation_Amos_Supuni_Woerden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1412" data-file-height="2040"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Reconciliation_Amos_Supuni_Woerden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>"Reconciliation", a <a href="/wiki/Stone_sculpture" title="Stone sculpture">stone sculpture</a> by <a href="/wiki/Amos_Supuni" title="Amos Supuni">Amos Supuni</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Traditional arts in Zimbabwe include <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basketry" class="mw-redirect" title="Basketry">basketry</a>, textiles, jewellery and carving. Among the distinctive qualities are symmetrically patterned woven baskets and stools carved out of a single piece of wood. Shona sculpture has become better known after finding initial popularity in the 1940s.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Most subjects of <a href="/wiki/Rock_carving" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock carving">carved</a> figures of stylised birds and human figures among others are made with <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary" class="mw-redirect" title="Sedimentary">sedimentary</a> rocks such as <a href="/wiki/Soapstone" title="Soapstone">soapstone</a>, as well as harder <a href="/wiki/Igneous" class="mw-redirect" title="Igneous">igneous</a> rocks such as <a href="/wiki/Serpentine_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Serpentine group">serpentine</a> and the rare stone <a href="/wiki/Serpentinite" title="Serpentinite">verdite</a>. Zimbabwean artefacts can be found in countries like Singapore, China and Canada. e.g. <a href="/wiki/Dominic_Benhura" title="Dominic Benhura">Dominic Benhura</a>'s statue in the <a href="/wiki/Singapore_Botanic_Gardens" title="Singapore Botanic Gardens">Singapore Botanic Gardens</a>. </p>
      <p>Shona sculpture has survived through the ages and the modern style is a fusion of African <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a> with European influences. World-renowned Zimbabwean sculptors include Nicholas, Nesbert and Anderson Mukomberanwa, Tapfuma Gutsa, Henry Munyaradzi and Locardia Ndandarika. Internationally, Zimbabwean sculptors have managed to influence a new generation of artists, particularly Black Americans, through lengthy apprenticeships with master sculptors in Zimbabwe. Contemporary artists like New York sculptor M. Scott Johnson and California sculptor Russel Albans have learned to fuse both African and Afro-diasporic aesthetics in a way that travels beyond the simplistic mimicry of African Art by some Black artists of past generations in the United States. </p>
      <p>Several authors are well known within Zimbabwe and abroad. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Mungoshi" title="Charles Mungoshi">Charles Mungoshi</a> is renowned in Zimbabwe for writing traditional stories in English and in Shona and his poems and books have sold well with both the black and white communities.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Buckle" title="Catherine Buckle">Catherine Buckle</a> has achieved international recognition with her two books <i>African Tears</i> and <i>Beyond Tears</i> which tell of the ordeal she went through under the 2000 <a href="/wiki/Land_reform_in_Zimbabwe" title="Land reform in Zimbabwe">Land Reform</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Rhodesia" title="Prime Minister of Rhodesia">Prime Minister of Rhodesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ian_Smith" title="Ian Smith">Ian Smith</a>, wrote two books – <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Betrayal:_The_Memoirs_of_Ian_Douglas_Smith" title="The Great Betrayal: The Memoirs of Ian Douglas Smith">The Great Betrayal</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Bitter_Harvest_(2001_book)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bitter Harvest (2001 book)">Bitter Harvest</a>.</i> The book <i><a href="/wiki/The_House_of_Hunger" title="The House of Hunger">The House of Hunger</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Dambudzo_Marechera" title="Dambudzo Marechera">Dambudzo Marechera</a> won an award in the UK in 1979 and the Nobel Prize-winning author <a href="/wiki/Doris_Lessing" title="Doris Lessing">Doris Lessing</a>'s first novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Grass_Is_Singing" title="The Grass Is Singing">The Grass Is Singing</a></i>, the first four volumes of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Children_of_Violence" class="mw-redirect" title="The Children of Violence">The Children of Violence</a></i> sequence, as well as the collection of short stories <i>African Stories</i> are set in Rhodesia. In 2013 NoViolet Bulawayo's novel <i>We Need New Names</i> was shortlisted for the <a href="/wiki/Booker_Prize" title="Booker Prize">Booker Prize</a>. The novel tells the story of the devastation and emigration caused by the brutal suppression of Zimbabwean civilians during the <a href="/wiki/Gukurahundi" title="Gukurahundi">Gukurahundi</a> in the early 1980s.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Notable artists include Henry Mudzengerere and Nicolas Mukomberanwa. A recurring theme in Zimbabwean art is the <a href="/wiki/Metamorphosis" title="Metamorphosis">metamorphosis</a> of man into beast.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> Zimbabwean musicians like <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mapfumo" title="Thomas Mapfumo">Thomas Mapfumo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Mtukudzi" title="Oliver Mtukudzi">Oliver Mtukudzi</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bhundu_Boys" title="Bhundu Boys">Bhundu Boys</a>; <a href="/wiki/Stella_Chiweshe" title="Stella Chiweshe">Stella Chiweshe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alick_Macheso" title="Alick Macheso">Alick Macheso</a> and <a href="/wiki/Audius_Mtawarira" title="Audius Mtawarira">Audius Mtawarira</a> have achieved international recognition. Among members of the white minority community, Theatre has a large following, with numerous theatrical companies performing in Zimbabwe's urban areas.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Goat_Offal.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Goat_Offal.JPG/220px-Goat_Offal.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Goat_Offal.JPG/330px-Goat_Offal.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Goat_Offal.JPG/440px-Goat_Offal.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4352" data-file-height="3264"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Goat_Offal.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A meal of <i>sadza</i> <i>(right)</i>, greens, and goat <a href="/wiki/Offal" title="Offal">offal</a>. The goat's small intestines are wrapped around small pieces of large intestines before cooking.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Like in many African countries, the majority of Zimbabweans depend on a few staple foods. "Mealie meal", also known as <a href="/wiki/Cornmeal" title="Cornmeal">cornmeal</a>, is used to prepare <i><a href="/wiki/Sadza" class="mw-redirect" title="Sadza">sadza</a></i> or <i>isitshwala</i>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Porridge" title="Porridge">porridge</a> known as <i>bota</i> or <i>ilambazi</i>. <i>Sadza</i> is made by mixing the cornmeal with water to produce a thick paste/porridge. After the paste has been cooking for several minutes, more cornmeal is added to thicken the paste. </p>
      <p>This is usually eaten as lunch or dinner, usually with sides such as <a href="/wiki/Gravy" title="Gravy">gravy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetables</a> (<a href="/wiki/Spinach" title="Spinach">spinach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chomolia" title="Chomolia">chomolia</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Spring_greens" title="Spring greens">spring greens</a>/<a href="/wiki/Collard_greens" class="mw-redirect" title="Collard greens">collard greens</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">beans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meat</a> (stewed, grilled, roasted, or sundried). <i>Sadza</i> is also commonly eaten with curdled milk (<a href="/wiki/Sour_milk" class="mw-redirect" title="Sour milk">sour milk</a>), commonly known as "lacto" (<i>mukaka wakakora</i>), or dried <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika_sardine" class="mw-redirect" title="Tanganyika sardine">Tanganyika sardine</a>, known locally as <i>kapenta</i> or <i>matemba</i>. <i>Bota</i> is a thinner porridge, cooked without the additional cornmeal and usually flavoured with <a href="/wiki/Peanut_butter" title="Peanut butter">peanut butter</a>, milk, butter, or <a href="/wiki/Jam" class="mw-redirect" title="Jam">jam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> <i>Bota</i> is usually eaten for breakfast. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Graduation" title="Graduation">Graduations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wedding" title="Wedding">weddings</a>, and any other family gatherings will usually be celebrated with the killing of a goat or cow, which will be barbecued or <a href="/wiki/Roasting" title="Roasting">roasted</a> by the family. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Boerewors_raw.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Boerewors_raw.jpg/220px-Boerewors_raw.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Boerewors_raw.jpg/330px-Boerewors_raw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Boerewors_raw.jpg/440px-Boerewors_raw.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="338"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Boerewors_raw.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Raw <i><a href="/wiki/Boerewors" title="Boerewors">boerewors</a></i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Even though the <a href="/wiki/Afrikaner" class="mw-redirect" title="Afrikaner">Afrikaners</a> are a small group (10%) within the white minority group, Afrikaner recipes are popular. <i><a href="/wiki/Biltong" title="Biltong">Biltong</a></i>, a type of <a href="/wiki/Jerky_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jerky (food)">jerky</a>, is a popular snack, prepared by hanging bits of spiced raw meat to dry in the shade.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Boerewors" title="Boerewors">Boerewors</a></i> is served with <i>sadza</i>. It is a long sausage, often well-spiced, composed of beef rather than pork, and barbecued.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>As Zimbabwe was a British colony, some people there have adopted some colonial-era English eating habits. For example, most people will have <a href="/wiki/Porridge" title="Porridge">porridge</a> in the morning, as well as 10 o'clock tea (midday tea). They will have lunch, often leftovers from the night before, freshly cooked <i>sadza</i>, or sandwiches (which is more common in the cities). After lunch, there is usually 4 o'clock tea (afternoon tea), which is served before dinner. It is not uncommon for <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a> to be had after dinner.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Rice, pasta, and <a href="/wiki/Potato_production_in_Zimbabwe" title="Potato production in Zimbabwe">potato-based foods</a> (<a href="/wiki/French_fries" title="French fries">french fries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mashed_potato" title="Mashed potato">mashed potato</a>) also make up part of Zimbabwean cuisine. A local favourite is rice cooked with peanut butter, which is taken with thick gravy, mixed vegetables and meat.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A potpourri of peanuts known as <i>nzungu</i>, boiled and sundried maize, <a href="/wiki/Black-eyed_pea" title="Black-eyed pea">black-eyed peas</a> known as <i>nyemba</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Bambara_groundnut" class="mw-redirect" title="Bambara groundnut">bambara groundnuts</a> known as <i>nyimo</i> makes a traditional dish called <i>mutakura</i>. <i>Mutakura</i> can also be the above ingredients cooked individually. </p>
      <p>One can also find local snacks, such as <i>maputi</i> (roasted/popped maize kernels similar to <a href="/wiki/Popcorn" title="Popcorn">popcorn</a>), roasted and salted <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanuts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">sweet potato</a>, pumpkin, and indigenous fruits, such as <a href="/wiki/Horned_melon" class="mw-redirect" title="Horned melon">horned melon</a>, <i>gaka</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Adansonia" title="Adansonia">adansonia</a></i>, <i>mawuyu</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Uapaca_kirkiana" title="Uapaca kirkiana">uapaca kirkiana</a></i>, <i>mazhanje</i> (sugar plum), and many others.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Zimbabwe" title="Sport in Zimbabwe">Sport in Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Canada_wins_Zimbabwe_in_Rio_Olympics_07.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Canada_wins_Zimbabwe_in_Rio_Olympics_07.jpg/220px-Canada_wins_Zimbabwe_in_Rio_Olympics_07.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Canada_wins_Zimbabwe_in_Rio_Olympics_07.jpg/330px-Canada_wins_Zimbabwe_in_Rio_Olympics_07.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Canada_wins_Zimbabwe_in_Rio_Olympics_07.jpg/440px-Canada_wins_Zimbabwe_in_Rio_Olympics_07.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4928" data-file-height="3264"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Canada_wins_Zimbabwe_in_Rio_Olympics_07.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_women%27s_national_football_team" title="Zimbabwe women's national football team">Zimbabwe women's national football team</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Football_at_the_2016_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Women%27s_tournament" title="Football at the 2016 Summer Olympics – Women's tournament">2016 Olympic Games</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">Football</a> <i>(also known as soccer)</i> is the most popular sport in Zimbabwe.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_national_football_team" title="Zimbabwe national football team">The Warriors</a> have qualified for the <a href="/wiki/Africa_Cup_of_Nations" title="Africa Cup of Nations">Africa Cup of Nations</a> five times (2004, 2006, 2017, 2019, 2021), and won the <a href="/wiki/COSAFA_Cup" title="COSAFA Cup">Southern Africa championship</a> on six occasions (2000, 2003, 2005, 2009, 2017, 2018) and the <a href="/wiki/CECAFA_Cup" title="CECAFA Cup">Eastern Africa cup</a> once (1985). The team is ranked 115th in the world (Fifa World Rankings Nov 2018). </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">Rugby union</a> is a significant sport in Zimbabwe. <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_national_rugby_union_team" title="Zimbabwe national rugby union team">The national side</a> have represented the country at 2 <a href="/wiki/Rugby_World_Cup" title="Rugby World Cup">Rugby World Cup</a> tournaments in 1987 and 1991. The team are ranked 26 in the world by World Rugby.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">Cricket</a> also has a following among the white minority. It is one of twelve <a href="/wiki/Test_cricket" title="Test cricket">Test cricket</a> playing nations and an <a href="/wiki/International_Cricket_Council" title="International Cricket Council">ICC</a> full member as well. Notable cricket players from Zimbabwe include <a href="/wiki/Andy_Flower" title="Andy Flower">Andy Flower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heath_Streak" title="Heath Streak">Heath Streak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brendan_Taylor" title="Brendan Taylor">Brendan Taylor</a>. </p>
      <p>Zimbabwe has won eight Olympic medals, one in <a href="/wiki/Field_hockey" title="Field hockey">field hockey</a> <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_women%27s_national_field_hockey_team" title="Zimbabwe women's national field hockey team">with the women's team</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_at_the_1980_Summer_Olympics" title="Zimbabwe at the 1980 Summer Olympics">1980 Summer Olympics</a> in Moscow, and seven by swimmer <a href="/wiki/Kirsty_Coventry" title="Kirsty Coventry">Kirsty Coventry</a>, three at the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_at_the_2004_Summer_Olympics" title="Zimbabwe at the 2004 Summer Olympics">2004 Summer Olympics</a> and four at the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_at_the_2008_Summer_Olympics" title="Zimbabwe at the 2008 Summer Olympics">2008 Summer Olympics</a>. </p>
      <p>Zimbabwe has also done well in the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games" title="Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a> and <a href="/wiki/All-Africa_Games" class="mw-redirect" title="All-Africa Games">All-Africa Games</a> in swimming with Kirsty Coventry obtaining 11 gold medals in the different competitions.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup> Zimbabwe has also competed at <a href="/wiki/The_Championships,_Wimbledon" title="The Championships, Wimbledon">Wimbledon</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Davis_Cup" title="Davis Cup">Davis Cup</a> in tennis, most notably with the Black family, which comprises <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Black" title="Wayne Black">Wayne Black</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byron_Black" title="Byron Black">Byron Black</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cara_Black" title="Cara Black">Cara Black</a>. Zimbabwe has also done well in golf. The Zimbabwean <a href="/wiki/Nick_Price" title="Nick Price">Nick Price</a> held the official World Number 1 status longer than any player from Africa has ever done in the 24-year history of the ranking.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Other sports played in Zimbabwe are basketball, volleyball, <a href="/wiki/Netball" title="Netball">netball</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Water_polo" title="Water polo">water polo</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Squash_(sport)" title="Squash (sport)">squash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Motorsport" title="Motorsport">motorsport</a>, martial arts, <a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">chess</a>, cycling, <a href="/wiki/Polocrosse" title="Polocrosse">polocrosse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kayaking" title="Kayaking">kayaking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horse_racing" title="Horse racing">horse racing</a>. However, most of these sports do not have international representatives but instead stay at a junior or national level. </p>
      <p>Zimbabwean professional <a href="/wiki/Rugby_league" title="Rugby league">rugby league</a> players playing overseas are <a href="/wiki/Masimbaashe_Motongo" class="mw-redirect" title="Masimbaashe Motongo">Masimbaashe Motongo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judah_Mazive" title="Judah Mazive">Judah Mazive</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup> Former players include now <a href="/wiki/SANZAAR" title="SANZAAR">SANZAAR</a> CEO <a href="/wiki/Andy_Marinos" title="Andy Marinos">Andy Marinos</a> who made an appearance for <a href="/wiki/South_Africa_national_rugby_league_team" title="South Africa national rugby league team">South Africa</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Super_League_World_Nines" title="Super League World Nines">Super League World Nines</a> and featured for the <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Bulldogs" class="mw-redirect" title="Sydney Bulldogs">Sydney Bulldogs</a> as well as Zimbabwe-born former <a href="/wiki/Scotland_national_rugby_union_team" title="Scotland national rugby union team">Scotland rugby union international</a> <a href="/wiki/Scott_Gray_(rugby_union)" title="Scott Gray (rugby union)">Scott Gray</a>, who spent time at the <a href="/wiki/Brisbane_Broncos" title="Brisbane Broncos">Brisbane Broncos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Media_of_Zimbabwe" class="mw-redirect" title="Media of Zimbabwe">Media of Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The media of Zimbabwe is now once again diverse, having come under tight restriction between 2002 and 2008 by the government during the growing economic and political crisis in the country. The Zimbabwean constitution promises freedom of the media and expression. Since the appointment of a new media and information minister in 2013 the media is facing less political interference and the supreme court has ruled some sections of the strict media laws as unconstitutional.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup> In July 2009 the BBC and CNN were able to resume operations and report legally and openly from Zimbabwe. CNN welcomed the move. The Zimbabwe Ministry of Media, Information and Publicity stated that, "the Zimbabwe government never banned the BBC from carrying out lawful activities inside Zimbabwe".<sup id="cite_ref-Telegraph_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph-153">[153]</a></sup> The BBC also welcomed the move saying, "we're pleased at being able to operate openly in Zimbabwe once again".<sup id="cite_ref-BBC1_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC1-271">[271]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2010 the Zimbabwe Media Commission was established by the inclusive, power-sharing government. In May 2010 the Commission licensed three new privately owned newspapers, including the previously banned <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_News_(Harare)" title="Daily News (Harare)">Daily News</a></i>, for publication.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-272">[272]</a></sup> Reporters Without Borders described the decisions as a "major advance".<sup id="cite_ref-IFEX_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IFEX-273">[273]</a></sup> In June 2010 <i><a href="/wiki/NewsDay_(Zimbabwean_newspaper)" title="NewsDay (Zimbabwean newspaper)">NewsDay</a></i> became the first independent daily newspaper to be published in Zimbabwe in seven years.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuter_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuter-274">[274]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>ZBC's monopoly in the broadcasting sector was ended with the licensing of two private radio stations in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Since the 2002 Access to Information and Protection of Privacy Act (AIPPA) was passed, a number of privately owned news outlets were shut down by the government, including <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_News_(Harare)" title="Daily News (Harare)">Daily News</a></i> whose managing director Wilf Mbanga went on to form the influential <a href="/wiki/The_Zimbabwean" title="The Zimbabwean">The Zimbabwean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rwb_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rwb-276">[276]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cgmg_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cgmg-277">[277]</a></sup> As a result, many press organisations have been set up in both neighbouring and Western countries by <a href="/wiki/Exile" title="Exile">exiled</a> Zimbabweans. Because the internet is unrestricted, many Zimbabweans are allowed to access online news sites set up by exiled journalists.<sup id="cite_ref-fh_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fh-278">[278]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Reporters_Without_Borders" title="Reporters Without Borders">Reporters Without Borders</a> claims the media environment in Zimbabwe involves "surveillance, threats, <a href="/wiki/Imprison" class="mw-redirect" title="Imprison">imprisonment</a>, censorship, <a href="/wiki/Blackmail" title="Blackmail">blackmail</a>, abuse of power and denial of <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">justice</a> are all brought to bear to keep firm control over the news."<sup id="cite_ref-rwb_276-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rwb-276">[276]</a></sup> The main published newspapers are <a href="/wiki/The_Herald_(Zimbabwe)" title="The Herald (Zimbabwe)"><i>The Herald</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Chronicle_(Zimbabwe)" title="The Chronicle (Zimbabwe)"><i>The Chronicle</i></a> which are printed in Harare and Bulawayo respectively. The heavy-handedness on the media has progressively relaxed since 2009. </p>
      <p>In its 2019 report, <a href="/wiki/Reporters_Without_Borders" title="Reporters Without Borders">Reporters Without Borders</a> ranked the Zimbabwean media as 127th out of 180.<sup id="cite_ref-rwb_276-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rwb-276">[276]</a></sup> The government also bans many foreign broadcasting stations from Zimbabwe, including the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Canadian Broadcasting Corporation">CBC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sky_News" title="Sky News">Sky News</a>, <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">Channel 4</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">American Broadcasting Company</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Australian Broadcasting Corporation">Australian Broadcasting Corporation (ABC)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fox_News" title="Fox News">Fox News</a>. News agencies and newspapers from other Western countries and South Africa have also been banned from the country. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scouting">Scouting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Scouting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Scout_Association_of_Zimbabwe" title="The Scout Association of Zimbabwe">The Scout Association of Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Burnham_sketch_by_baden-powell.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Burnham_sketch_by_baden-powell.jpg/170px-Burnham_sketch_by_baden-powell.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Burnham_sketch_by_baden-powell.jpg/255px-Burnham_sketch_by_baden-powell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Burnham_sketch_by_baden-powell.jpg/340px-Burnham_sketch_by_baden-powell.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="576"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Burnham_sketch_by_baden-powell.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Robert_Baden-Powell,_1st_Baron_Baden-Powell" title="Robert Baden-Powell, 1st Baron Baden-Powell">Baden-Powell</a>'s drawing of Chief of Scouts <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Russell_Burnham" title="Frederick Russell Burnham">Burnham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matobo_Hills" class="mw-redirect" title="Matobo Hills">Matobo Hills</a>, 1896
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>It was in the <a href="/wiki/Matabeleland" title="Matabeleland">Matabeleland</a> region in Zimbabwe that, during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Matabele_War" title="Second Matabele War">Second Matabele War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Baden-Powell,_1st_Baron_Baden-Powell" title="Robert Baden-Powell, 1st Baron Baden-Powell">Robert Baden-Powell</a>, the founder of <a href="/wiki/Scouting" title="Scouting">Scouting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Russell_Burnham" title="Frederick Russell Burnham">Frederick Russell Burnham</a>, the American-born Chief of Scouts for the British Army, first met and began their lifelong friendship.<sup id="cite_ref-scouting_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scouting-279">[279]</a></sup> In mid-June 1896, during a scouting patrol in the <a href="/wiki/Matobo_Hills" class="mw-redirect" title="Matobo Hills">Matobo Hills</a>, Burnham began teaching Baden-Powell <a href="/wiki/Woodcraft" title="Woodcraft">woodcraft</a>. Baden-Powell and Burnham discussed the concept of a broad training programme in woodcraft for young men, rich in exploration, <a href="/wiki/Tracking_(hunting)" title="Tracking (hunting)">tracking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fieldcraft" title="Fieldcraft">fieldcraft</a>, and self-reliance.<sup id="cite_ref-vanwyk_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanwyk-280">[280]</a></sup> It was also during this time in the Matobo Hills that Baden-Powell first started to wear his signature <a href="/wiki/Campaign_hat" title="Campaign hat">campaign hat</a> like the one worn by Burnham.<sup id="cite_ref-jeal_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jeal-281">[281]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Scouting in the former <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia_and_Nyasaland" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhodesia and Nyasaland">Rhodesia and Nyasaland</a> started in 1909 when the first Boy Scout troop was registered. Scouting grew quickly and in 1924 Rhodesia and Nyasaland sent a large contingent to the second <a href="/wiki/World_Scout_Jamboree" title="World Scout Jamboree">World Scout Jamboree</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ermelunden,_Denmark" title="Ermelunden, Denmark">Ermelunden, Denmark</a>. In 1959, Rhodesia hosted the Central African Jamboree at <a href="/wiki/Ruwa" title="Ruwa">Ruwa</a>. In 2009, Scouts celebrated 100 years of Scouting in Zimbabwe and hundreds of Scouts camped at <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Park,_Zimbabwe" class="mw-redirect" title="Gordon Park, Zimbabwe">Gordon Park</a>, a Scout campground and training area, as part of these celebrations.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Besides scouting, there are also leadership, life skills and general knowledge courses and training experiences mainly for schoolchildren ranging from pre-school to final year high school students and sometimes those beyond high school. These courses and outings are held at, for example, <i>Lasting Impressions</i> (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Q5GiuX0O_so"><span class="plainlinks">Lasting Impressions ~Zimbabwe</span></a> on <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a>), <i>Far and Wide Zimbabwe</i> (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.farandwide.co.zw/">Far and wide.</a>) and <i>Chimanimani Outward Bound</i> (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070616100110/http://www.outwardbound.net/locations/zimbabwe.php">Outwardbound Zimbabwe</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> (archived 16 June 2007)). </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_symbols">National symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: National symbols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Zimbabwe_Bird.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Zimbabwe_Bird.svg/170px-Zimbabwe_Bird.svg.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Zimbabwe_Bird.svg/255px-Zimbabwe_Bird.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Zimbabwe_Bird.svg/340px-Zimbabwe_Bird.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="744" data-file-height="1052"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Zimbabwe_Bird.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Traditional Zimbabwe Bird design
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The stone-carved <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Bird" title="Zimbabwe Bird">Zimbabwe Bird</a> appears on the national flags and the coats of arms of both Zimbabwe and <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a>, as well as on <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_dollar" title="Rhodesian dollar">banknotes</a> and coins (first on <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_Rhodesian_pound" title="Coins of the Rhodesian pound">Rhodesian pound</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_Rhodesian_dollar" title="Coins of the Rhodesian dollar">Rhodesian dollar</a>). It probably represents the <a href="/wiki/Bateleur" title="Bateleur">bateleur eagle</a> or the <a href="/wiki/African_fish_eagle" title="African fish eagle">African fish eagle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The famous <a href="/wiki/Soapstone" title="Soapstone">soapstone</a> bird carvings stood on walls and monoliths of the ancient city of <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe</a>, built, it is believed, sometime between the 13th and 16th centuries by ancestors of the <a href="/wiki/Shona_people" title="Shona people">Shona</a>. The ruins, which gave their name to modern Zimbabwe, cover some 730 hectares (1,800 acres) and are the largest ancient stone construction in Zimbabwe.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Balancing_Rocks" title="Balancing Rocks">Balancing Rocks</a> are geological formations all over Zimbabwe. The rocks are perfectly balanced without other supports. They are created when ancient granite intrusions are exposed to weathering, as softer rocks surrounding them erode away. They are often remarked on and have been depicted on both the <a href="/wiki/Banknotes_of_Zimbabwe" title="Banknotes of Zimbabwe">banknotes of Zimbabwe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_dollar" title="Rhodesian dollar">Rhodesian dollar</a> banknotes. The ones found on the current notes of Zimbabwe, named the Banknote Rocks, are located in <a href="/wiki/Epworth,_Zimbabwe" title="Epworth, Zimbabwe">Epworth</a>, approximately 14&nbsp;km (9&nbsp;mi) south east of Harare.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup> There are many different formations of the rocks, incorporating single and paired columns of 3 or more rocks. These formations are a feature of south and east tropical Africa from northern South Africa northwards to Sudan. The most notable formations in Zimbabwe are located in the <a href="/wiki/Matobo_National_Park" title="Matobo National Park">Matobo National Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Matabeleland" title="Matabeleland">Matabeleland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica.com_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-287">[287]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Anthem_of_Zimbabwe" title="National Anthem of Zimbabwe">National Anthem of Zimbabwe</a> is "Blessed be the Land of Zimbabwe" (<a href="/wiki/Shona_language" title="Shona language">Shona</a>: <i lang="sn">"Simudzai Mureza wedu WeZimbabwe"</i>; <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ndebele_language" title="Northern Ndebele language">Northern Ndebele</a>: <i lang="nd">"Kalibusiswe Ilizwe leZimbabwe"</i>). It was introduced in March 1994 after a nationwide competition to replace "<i lang="sn" title="Shona-language text"><a href="/wiki/Ishe_Komborera_Africa" title="Ishe Komborera Africa">Ishe Komborera Africa</a></i>" as a distinctly Zimbabwean song. The winning entry was a song written by Professor <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Mutswairo" title="Solomon Mutswairo">Solomon Mutswairo</a> and composed by Fred Changundega. It has been translated into all three of the main languages of Zimbabwe.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica.com_287-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-287">[287]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_Zimbabwe" title="HIV/AIDS in Zimbabwe">HIV/AIDS in Zimbabwe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_cholera_outbreak" class="mw-redirect" title="Zimbabwean cholera outbreak">Zimbabwean cholera outbreak</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:MOP_50.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/MOP_50.jpg/220px-MOP_50.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/MOP_50.jpg/330px-MOP_50.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/MOP_50.jpg/440px-MOP_50.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:MOP_50.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Mother of Peace AIDS orphanage, <a href="/wiki/Mutoko" title="Mutoko">Mutoko</a> (2005)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>At independence, the policies of racial inequality were reflected in the disease patterns of the black majority. The first five years after independence saw rapid gains in areas such as immunisation coverage, access to health care, and contraceptive prevalence rate.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup> Zimbabwe was thus considered internationally to have an achieved a good record of health development.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Zimbabwe suffered occasional outbreaks of acute diseases (such as <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plague</a> in 1994). The gains on the national health were eroded by structural adjustment in the 1990s,<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup> the impact of the <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_Zimbabwe" title="HIV/AIDS in Zimbabwe">HIV/AIDS pandemic</a><sup id="cite_ref-nofix_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nofix-163">[163]</a></sup> and the economic crisis since the year 2000. In 2006, Zimbabwe had one of the lowest life expectancies in the world according to UN figure—44 for men and 43 for women, down from 60 in 1990, but recovered to 60 in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-291">[291]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup> The rapid drop was ascribed mainly to the HIV/AIDS pandemic. <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">Infant mortality</a> rose from 6% in the late 1990s to 12.3% by 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-nofix_163-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nofix-163">[163]</a></sup> By 2016 HIV/AIDS prevalence had been reduced to 13.5%<sup id="cite_ref-:2_291-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-291">[291]</a></sup> compared to 40% in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-Worldbank_196-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worldbank-196">[196]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The health system has more or less collapsed. At the end of November 2008, some operations at three of Zimbabwe's four major referral hospitals had shut down, along with the Zimbabwe Medical School, and the fourth major hospital had two <a href="/wiki/Hospital" title="Hospital">wards</a> and no <a href="/wiki/Operating_theatre" class="mw-redirect" title="Operating theatre">operating theatres</a> working.<sup id="cite_ref-hospitalsprnid_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hospitalsprnid-293">[293]</a></sup> Due to <a href="/wiki/Hyperinflation_in_Zimbabwe" title="Hyperinflation in Zimbabwe">hyperinflation</a>, those hospitals still open were not able to obtain basic drugs and medicines.<sup id="cite_ref-coping_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coping-294">[294]</a></sup> The situation changed drastically after the Unity Government and the introduction of the multi-currency system in February 2009 although the political and economic crisis also contributed to the emigration of the doctors and people with medical knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-Zimbabwe_cholera_deaths_near_500_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zimbabwe_cholera_deaths_near_500-295">[295]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:2008_Zimbabwe_Cholera_Outbreak_Combined.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/2008_Zimbabwe_Cholera_Outbreak_Combined.svg/220px-2008_Zimbabwe_Cholera_Outbreak_Combined.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/2008_Zimbabwe_Cholera_Outbreak_Combined.svg/330px-2008_Zimbabwe_Cholera_Outbreak_Combined.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/2008_Zimbabwe_Cholera_Outbreak_Combined.svg/440px-2008_Zimbabwe_Cholera_Outbreak_Combined.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="630" data-file-height="490"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:2008_Zimbabwe_Cholera_Outbreak_Combined.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Map showing the spread of <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> in and around Zimbabwe put together from several sources.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In August 2008 large areas of Zimbabwe were struck by the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> epidemic. By December 2008 more than 10,000 people had been infected in all but one of Zimbabwe's provinces and the outbreak had spread to Botswana, Mozambique, South Africa and Zambia.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Milliband_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Milliband-297">[297]</a></sup> On 4 December 2008 the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Zimbabwe" class="mw-redirect" title="Government of Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe government</a> declared the outbreak to be a national emergency and asked for international aid.<sup id="cite_ref-Zimbabwe_declares_national_emergency_over_cholera_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zimbabwe_declares_national_emergency_over_cholera-298">[298]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-emergency_299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emergency-299">[299]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By 9 March 2009 The World Health Organization (WHO) estimated that 4,011 people had succumbed to the waterborne disease since the outbreak began in August 2008, and the total number of cases recorded had reached 89,018.<sup id="cite_ref-On_the_cholera_frontline_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-On_the_cholera_frontline-300">[300]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Harare</a>, the city council offered free graves to cholera victims.<sup id="cite_ref-rain_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rain-301">[301]</a></sup> There had been signs that the disease is abating, with cholera infections down by about 50% to around 4,000 cases a week.<sup id="cite_ref-On_the_cholera_frontline_300-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-On_the_cholera_frontline-300">[300]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The 2014 maternal mortality rate per 100,000 births for Zimbabwe was 614<sup id="cite_ref-Worldbank_196-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worldbank-196">[196]</a></sup> compared to 960 in 2010–11<sup id="cite_ref-Worldbank_196-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worldbank-196">[196]</a></sup> and 232 in 1990. The under five mortality rate, per 1,000 births was 75 in 2014 (94 in 2009).<sup id="cite_ref-Worldbank_196-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worldbank-196">[196]</a></sup> The number of midwives per 1,000 live births was unavailable in 2016 and the lifetime risk of death for pregnant women 1 in 42.<sup id="cite_ref-SOWMY_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SOWMY-302">[302]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Zimbabwe" title="Education in Zimbabwe">Education in Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Saint-Georges-College.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Saint-Georges-College.jpg/220px-Saint-Georges-College.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Saint-Georges-College.jpg/330px-Saint-Georges-College.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/61/Saint-Georges-College.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="225"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Saint-Georges-College.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_College,_Harare" class="mw-redirect" title="St George's College, Harare">St George's College, Harare</a> was established in 1896 by a French <a href="/wiki/Jesuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Due to large investments in education since independence, Zimbabwe has the highest adult literacy rate in Africa which in 2013 was 90.70%.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">[304]</a></sup> This is lower than the 92% recorded in 2010 by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">United Nations Development Programme</a><sup id="cite_ref-IRINAfrica_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IRINAfrica-305">[305]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AllAfrica_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllAfrica-306">[306]</a></sup> and the 97.0% recorded in the 2002 census, while still substantially higher than 80.4% recorded in the 1992 census.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The education department has stated that 20,000 teachers have left Zimbabwe since 2007 and that half of Zimbabwe's children have not progressed beyond primary school.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The wealthier portion of the population usually send their children to independent schools as opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Government_school" class="mw-redirect" title="Government school">government-run</a> schools which are attended by the majority as these are subsidised by the government. School education was made free in 1980, but since 1988, the government has steadily increased the charges attached to school enrolment until they now greatly exceed the real value of fees in 1980. The Ministry of Education of Zimbabwe maintains and operates the government schools but the fees charged by independent schools are regulated by the cabinet of Zimbabwe. </p>
      <p>Zimbabwe's education system consists of two years of pre-school, seven years of primary and six years of secondary schooling before students can enter university in the country or abroad. The academic year in Zimbabwe runs from January to December, with three terms, broken up by one month holidays, with a total of 40 weeks of school per year. National examinations are written during the third term in November, with <a href="/wiki/General_Certificate_of_Education" title="General Certificate of Education">"O" level</a> and <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Level_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Advanced Level (UK)">"A" level</a> subjects also offered in June.<sup id="cite_ref-embassy_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embassy-309">[309]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There are seven public (Government) universities as well as four church-related universities in Zimbabwe that are fully internationally accredited.<sup id="cite_ref-embassy_309-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embassy-309">[309]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Zimbabwe" title="University of Zimbabwe">University of Zimbabwe</a>, the first and largest, was built in 1952 and is located in the Harare suburb of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Pleasant,_Harare" title="Mount Pleasant, Harare">Mount Pleasant</a>. Notable <a href="/wiki/Alumni" class="mw-redirect" title="Alumni">alumni</a> from Zimbabwean universities include <a href="/wiki/Welshman_Ncube" title="Welshman Ncube">Welshman Ncube</a>; Peter Moyo (of Amabhubesi); <a href="/wiki/Tendai_Biti" title="Tendai Biti">Tendai Biti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chenjerai_Hove" title="Chenjerai Hove">Chenjerai Hove</a>, Zimbabwean poet, novelist and essayist; and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Mutambara" title="Arthur Mutambara">Arthur Mutambara</a>. Many of the politicians in the government of Zimbabwe have obtained degrees from universities in USA or other universities abroad. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/National_University_of_Science_and_Technology,_Zimbabwe" title="National University of Science and Technology, Zimbabwe">National University of Science and Technology</a> (NUST) is the second largest public research university in Zimbabwe located in Bulawayo. It was established in 1991. The National University of Science and Technology strives to become a flourishing and reputable institution not only in Zimbabwe and in Southern Africa but also among the international fraternity of Universities. Its guidance, cultural values is the encouragement of all its members and society of those attitudes of fair mindedness, understanding, tolerance and respect for people and views which are essential for the attainment and maintenance of justice, peace and harmony at all times. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Africa_University" title="Africa University">Africa University</a> is a United Methodist related university institution located in Manicaland which attracts students from at least 36 African countries. The institution has been growing steadily and has steady study material and learning facilities. The highest professional board for accountants is the <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Chartered_Accountants_of_Zimbabwe" title="Institute of Chartered Accountants of Zimbabwe">Institute of Chartered Accountants of Zimbabwe</a> (ICAZ) with direct relationships with similar bodies in South Africa, Canada, the UK and Australia. A qualified <a href="/wiki/Chartered_Accountant" class="mw-redirect" title="Chartered Accountant">Chartered Accountant</a> from Zimbabwe is also a member of similar bodies in these countries after writing a conversion paper. In addition, Zimbabwean-trained doctors only require one year of residence to be fully licensed doctors in the United States. The <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_Institution_of_Engineers" title="Zimbabwe Institution of Engineers">Zimbabwe Institution of Engineers</a> (ZIE) is the highest professional board for engineers. </p>
      <p>Education in Zimbabwe became under threat since the economic changes in 2000 with teachers going on strike because of low pay, students unable to concentrate because of hunger and the price of uniforms soaring making this standard a luxury. Teachers were also one of the main targets of Mugabe's attacks because he thought they were not strong supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_equality">Gender equality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Gender equality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <table class="box-Undue-section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">
           <img alt="Unbalanced scales.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/45px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" decoding="async" width="45" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/68px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/90px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="354">

      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Child_marriage_in_Zimbabwe" title="Child marriage in Zimbabwe">Child marriage in Zimbabwe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polygamy_in_Zimbabwe" title="Polygamy in Zimbabwe">Polygamy in Zimbabwe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking_in_Zimbabwe" title="Human trafficking in Zimbabwe">Human trafficking in Zimbabwe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abortion_in_Zimbabwe" title="Abortion in Zimbabwe">Abortion in Zimbabwe</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Women in Zimbabwe are disadvantaged in many facets including economic, political, and social spheres, and experience Sex and Gender Based Violence.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-311">[311]</a></sup> A 2014 UN report found that deep rooted cultural issues, patriarchal attitudes, and religious practices negatively impacted women's rights and freedoms in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_311-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-311">[311]</a></sup> These negative views toward women as well as societal norms impact the incentive for women to participate in the economy and hinder their economic production.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_311-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-311">[311]</a></sup> Zimbabwe's constitution passed in 2013 has provisions in it that provide incentive to achieve greater gender equality but the data shows that enforcement has been lax and adoption slow.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_311-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-311">[311]</a></sup> In December 2016 the International Federation of Red Cross and Red Crescent Societies conducted a case study to determine how to best implement effective policy to address issues such as gender violence and implementation of equality laws.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-312">[312]</a></sup> It was found that Sex and Gender Based Violence against women and girls was increasing in areas that had experienced disasters (floods, drought, disease) but could not quantify the extent of the increase.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_312-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-312">[312]</a></sup> Some of the obstacles in combating these issues are that there are economic barriers to declaring SGBV to be unacceptable as well as social barriers.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_312-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-312">[312]</a></sup> Additionally, governmental services which were installed to help educate the populace about these issues as well as provide services to victims are underfunded and unable to carry out their duties.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_312-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-312">[312]</a></sup> The UN also provided economic incentive to adopt policies which would discourage these practices which negatively impacted women in Zimbabwe.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-313">[313]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discrimination_in_the_work_force">Discrimination in the work force</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Discrimination in the work force">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Zimbabwean women face cultural and social adversity in their professional lives which impacts their educational attainment, professional development, and advancement.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-314">[314]</a></sup> In 2009 the South African Journal of Education found that although the majority of primary school teachers in their random sample size were qualified for advancement to administrative positions, none of them had applied for administrative openings.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_314-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-314">[314]</a></sup> The women did not see themselves as equals with their male counterparts and believed their role as a wife and mother superseded all other parts of their lives.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_314-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-314">[314]</a></sup> The women surveyed in this trial were also found to have low self-esteem, a possible correlation to their societal roles and gender stereotypes.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_314-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-314">[314]</a></sup> In 2016 the FAO found that only 60% of women participated in the economy in some form compared to 74.3% for their male counterparts.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-315">[315]</a></sup> Women also made up the majority of low education jobs, such as 70% of the agriculture work force, yet only made up 16.7% and 21% of local authority and managers in the private sector respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_315-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-315">[315]</a></sup> In the public sector, women comprised 14% of the Zimbabwean House of Assembly and 33% of the Senate, despite the population ratio being 0.95 males per 1 female.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-316">[316]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:02_313-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-313">[313]</a></sup> To address gender inequality in the economy, the UN supports policies which help increase the number of women in leadership roles, such as heads of schools, with increased funding in line with #3 of the outlined Millennium Development Goals.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_313-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-313">[313]</a></sup> Through these policies Zimbabwe has made gains in closing the gender gap in school enrolment: 50.5% of males are enrolled in secondary schools compared to 49.5% in females.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_316-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-316">[316]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_violence">Domestic violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Domestic violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Zimbabwe experiences high rates of domestic and sexual violence; the Zimbabwe National Statistics Office shows that rates are increasing.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-317">[317]</a></sup> 21 rapes are reported per day in Zimbabwe - a rate of 0.12 rapes a day per 100,000 people. As not all rapes are reported, the actual number is likely higher. Reported rape increased 42% between 2010 and 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_317-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-317">[317]</a></sup> Of all the violence against girls and women reported in Zimbabwe, 78% was inflicted by their spouse, father, or domestic partner.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_317-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-317">[317]</a></sup> UNICEF reports show that one in three girls that grow up in Zimbabwe experience sexual assault before turning 18, this is further exacerbated by cultural norms such as child marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_317-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-317">[317]</a></sup> Young girls often run away with older men when their educational opportunities are limited or to escape a violent household.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_317-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-317">[317]</a></sup> These incidents of domestic violence or young girls running away with older men are usually not investigated by police as men are viewed as superior to women in Zimbabwean culture and their role as the dominant person in the relationship is to discipline their spouse, often violently.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_317-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-317">[317]</a></sup> There is an ingrained cultural norm that violence can be a show of power and love which makes ending domestic abuse in Zimbabwe difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_317-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-317">[317]</a></sup> The Zimbabwe Women's Lawyers Association is an organisation that is assisting the implementation of the legal framework, as defined in the 2013 constitution, to help women. The association provides programmes which help educate women on their rights and provides them with opportunities as a way of combating domestic and sexual violence.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_317-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-317">[317]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_representation">Political representation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Political representation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Women in Zimbabwe do not have proportional representation in the Zimbabwean lower and upper houses of Parliament holding 14% and 33% of seats respectively, despite being a slight majority of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_315-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-315">[315]</a></sup> There are cultural and violence barriers women have to overcome to run for public office; they are seen as "loose and immoral", called prostitutes, claimed to want to be men, and their private lives are heavily scrutinised.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-318">[318]</a></sup> Women seeking to participate in the political landscape as candidates or voters cite violence as one of the main reasons they are dissuaded from participating.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_318-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-318">[318]</a></sup> Lack of financial resources and confidence in their abilities stops many young women from attempting to run as well as preconceived notions about women in politics creates an environment that limits their involvement and desire to be involved in politics.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_319-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-319">[319]</a></sup> Women also make up a disproportionate amount of the rural poor in Zimbabwe and make up 70% of the agricultural work force. The rural poor find it difficult to access information and materials in relation to politics as well as travel to polling stations and become registered to vote.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_315-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-315">[315]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:8_319-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-319">[319]</a></sup> Collectively, women control 35% of parliamentary seats as a result of a provision of the 2013 constitution that mandated at least 30% of seats be occupied by women.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_320-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-320">[320]</a></sup> A 10-year extension is being considered to this mandate as it is only law until 2022 and equal representation according to population distribution has not yet been achieved.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_320-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-320">[320]</a></sup> A study by the Research and Advocacy Unit found that political parties in the country appoint women to "window dress" and not for their political advancement.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_320-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-320">[320]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Women in Zimbabwean society and culture are often seen as inferior, treated as objects, and viewed in subordinate roles in history and philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_321-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-321">[321]</a></sup> Ubuntu, an African philosophy's spiritual aspect instills the belief that boys should be more valued than girls as boys pass on lineage and the belief system places high value in respecting one's ancestors.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_321-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-321">[321]</a></sup> A common expression used in court, "<i>vakadzi ngavanyarare</i>", translates to "<i>women should keep quiet</i>" and as a result women are not consulted in decision-making; they must implement the men's wishes.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_321-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-321">[321]</a></sup> The subordination of women in Zimbabwe and the cultural forces which dictate what they must be, have led to deaths and the sacrifice of professional advancement in order for them to fulfill their roles as wives, mothers, and subordinates.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_314-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-314">[314]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:10_321-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-321">[321]</a></sup> Women are taught that they must never refuse their husband's sexual advances, even if they know they are infected with HIV from being unfaithful.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_321-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-321">[321]</a></sup> As a result of this practice, Zimbabwean women aged 15–49 have an HIV prevalence rate of 16.1% and make up 62% of the total population infected with HIV in that age group.<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">[322]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zimbabwe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite id="CITEREFMcGregorPrimorac2010" class="citation cs2">McGregor, JoAnn; Primorac, Ranka, eds. Scholarly essays on displacement as a result of Zimbabwe's continuing crisis, with a focus on diasporic communities in Britain and South Africa; also explores such topics as the revival of Rhodesian discourse.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Martin_Meredith" title="Martin Meredith">Meredith, Martin</a>. <i>Mugabe: Power, Plunder, and the Struggle for Zimbabwe's Future</i> (2007) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.amazon.com/dp/B003STCO04/">excerpt and text search</a>.</li> 
       <li>
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite id="CITEREFOrnerHolmes2011" class="citation cs2"><a href="/wiki/Peter_Orner" title="Peter Orner">Orner, Peter</a>; Holmes, Annie (2011), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.voiceofwitness.org/hope-deferred/"><i>Hope Deferred: Narratives of Zimbabwean Lives</i></a>, Voice of witness</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Hope+Deferred%3A+Narratives+of+Zimbabwean+Lives&amp;rft.pub=Voice+of+witness&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.aulast=Orner&amp;rft.aufirst=Peter&amp;rft.au=Holmes%2C+Annie&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.voiceofwitness.org%2Fhope-deferred%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AZimbabwe" class="Z3988"></span>.</li> 
       <li>Smith, Ian Douglas. <i>Bitter Harvest: Zimbabwe and the Aftermath of its Independence</i> (2008) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.amazon.com/dp/1857826043/">excerpt and text search</a>.</li>
      </ul> 
      <p>Fontein, Joost "Remaking Mutirikwi: Landscape, Water and belonging in Southern Zimbabwe" (2015), James Currey, BIEA Eastern African Series. </p> 
      <ul>
       <li>David Coltart. The struggle continues: 50 Years of Tyranny in Zimbabwe. Jacana Media (Pty) Ltd: South Africa, 2016.</li>

                     <div> 
                      <ul>
                       <li>1.&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Condominium_(international_law)" title="Condominium (international law)">Occupied jointly</a> with the United States.</li> 
                       <li>2.&nbsp;In 1931, Canada and other British <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominions</a> obtained self-government through the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Westminster_1931" title="Statute of Westminster 1931">Statute of Westminster</a>. See <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Canada" title="Name of Canada">Name of Canada</a>.</li> 
                       <li>3.&nbsp;Gave up <a href="/wiki/Responsible_government" title="Responsible government">self-rule</a> in 1934, but remained a <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> Dominion until it <a href="/wiki/Provinces_and_territories_of_Canada" title="Provinces and territories of Canada">joined Canada</a> in 1949.</li>

British Cameroons is now part of <i><a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a></i>, while Tanganyika is part of <i><a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a></i>.</li> 
                       <li>14.&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Responsible_government" title="Responsible government">Self-governing</a> Southern Rhodesia <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia%27s_Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Rhodesia's Unilateral Declaration of Independence">unilaterally declared independence</a> in 1965 (as <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a>) and continued as an <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_recognition" title="Diplomatic recognition">unrecognised</a> state until the 1979 <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_House_Agreement" title="Lancaster House Agreement">Lancaster House Agreement</a>. After recognised independence in 1980, Zimbabwe was a member of the Commonwealth until it withdrew in 2003.</li>

                       <li>20.&nbsp;Now part of the *<i><a href="/wiki/Realm_of_New_Zealand" title="Realm of New Zealand">Realm of New Zealand</a></i>.</li> 
                       <li>21. Now <i><a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">Vanuatu</a></i>.</li> 
                       <li>22.&nbsp;Now <i><a href="/wiki/Kiribati" title="Kiribati">Kiribati</a></i> and *<i><a href="/wiki/Tuvalu" title="Tuvalu">Tuvalu</a></i>.</li>
                      </ul> 
                     </div></td>
                   </tr>
                  </tbody>
                 </table>

               </tr>

                    <th scope="col" class="navbox-title" colspan="2">
                     <div id="Antarctica_and_South_Atlantic" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em">
                      Antarctica and South Atlantic
                     </div></th>
                   </tr>
                   <tr>
                    <td colspan="2" class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
                     <div style="padding:0em 0.25em"> 
                      <ul>
                       <li>Since 1658 <u><a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">Saint Helena</a></u><sup>23</sup></li> 
                       <li>Since 1815 <u><a href="/wiki/Ascension_Island" title="Ascension Island">Ascension Island</a></u><sup>23</sup></li> 
                       <li>Since 1816 <u><a href="/wiki/Tristan_da_Cunha" title="Tristan da Cunha">Tristan da Cunha</a></u><sup>23</sup></li> 
                       <li>Since 1833 <u><a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a></u></li> 
                       <li>1841–1933 <i><a href="/wiki/Australian_Antarctic_Territory" title="Australian Antarctic Territory">Australian Antarctic Territory</a></i> <small>(transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Commonwealth of Australia">Commonwealth of Australia</a>)</small></li> 
                       <li>1841–1947 <i><a href="/wiki/Ross_Dependency" title="Ross Dependency">Ross Dependency</a></i> <small>(transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Realm_of_New_Zealand" title="Realm of New Zealand">Realm of New Zealand</a>)</small></li> 
                       <li>Since 1908 <u><a href="/wiki/British_Antarctic_Territory" title="British Antarctic Territory">British Antarctic Territory</a></u><sup>24</sup></li> 
                       <li>Since 1908 <u><a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands</a></u><sup>24</sup></li>
                      </ul> 
                     </div></td>
                   </tr>
                   <tr>
                    <td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2">
                     <div> 
                      <ul>
                       <li>23.&nbsp;Since 2009 part of <u><a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena,_Ascension_and_Tristan_da_Cunha" title="Saint Helena, Ascension and Tristan da Cunha">Saint Helena, Ascension and Tristan da Cunha</a></u>; Ascension Island (1922–) and Tristan da Cunha (1938–) were previously dependencies of Saint Helena.</li> 
                       <li>24.&nbsp;Both claimed in 1908; territories formed in 1962 (British Antarctic Territory) and 1985 (South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands).</li>
                      </ul>
